Types of Isti'ara: Asliyya and Tab'iyya
Defining and distinguishing between original and subsidiary metaphor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between asliyya and tab'iyya isti'ara and apply the rule of dependency to identify subsidiary metaphor in derivatives and particles.

Core Idea
Subsidiary metaphor always follows a prior metaphor in the source word — never stands alone.

In Hanafi rhetorical analysis (balaghah), metaphors are classified into two fundamental types: original (asliyya) in generic nouns and subsidiary (tab'iyya) in derivatives and particles. This distinction is crucial for understanding how linguistic devices acquire figurative meanings in legal texts. This lesson unpacks the definitions, illustrates the dependency, and demonstrates the analytical chain.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types
Recognize that isti'ara asliyya occurs in generic nouns (asma' al-ajnas) such as solid nouns and masdars, while isti'ara tab'iyya occurs in derivatives (mushtaqqat) and particles (huruf).
Generic nouns include masdars and independent noun meanings attached to particles.
2
Understand the dependency
Tab'iyya is called subsidiary because it cannot occur independently; it requires a prior metaphor in the source (masdar). The derivative metaphor is built upon the source metaphor.
3
Analyze the derivative example
Take the phrase 'al-hal natiqa'. The word 'natiqa' (speaking) is derived from 'natiq' (speech). First, a metaphor of 'speaking' is used for 'indicating'. Then, the derivative 'natiqa' is used metaphorically for 'indicating' following that source metaphor.
4
Analyze the particle example
For particles like 'lam' (for causation), the process is similar: First, the meaning of causation is metaphorically used for 'following' (ta'qib). Then, the particle 'lam' is used metaphorically for 'following'. The example: 'li-daw al-mawt' where death follows birth.
5
Apply in usul al-fiqh
Recognizing whether a metaphor is asliyya or tab'iyya helps in interpreting legal language. For instance, understanding that a verb or particle is used tab'iyya requires tracing the base metaphor to determine the intended meaning.

مشتقات اور حروف میں ہوتا ہے اسم جنس سے مراد اسم جامد جو دال على المعنی الجنسی ہو اور مصدر جو مشتق منہ ہوتی ہے اور اسی طرح معنی حرفی کے متعلقات جیسے من لا بتد ا لغایہ اور لام للتعليل تو یہ ابتدا اور پھر بواسطہ استعارة التعليل للتعقيب یستعار اللام لام کا استعارہ برائے تعقیب ہوگا نحو لد و الموت وابنو اللخراب چونکہ موت ولادة کے بعد اور عقب میں ہوتی ہے تو جعل كان الولادة تو گویا کہ ولادۃ کو موت کے لئے علہ قرار دیا گیا نا ستعمل لام التعلیان لام تعلیل استعمال کر کے ارادہ کیا گیا کہ موت ولادت کے بعد یقیناً واقع ہوگی بلا تخلف وتوع المعلول جیسے معلول کا وقوع بعد از علت قطعی ہوتا ہے ایسے وابنو اللخراب میں کیونکہ کسی دار کا ویران اور خراب ہونا بعد از بنا ہوتا ہے جیسے موت بعد از ولادت تھی رحن ابناء على ان اللام اور یہ اس پر مبنی ہے کہ لام علہ غانیہ پر داخل ہوتا اور یہی غایتہ غرض من الفعل ہوتی ہے ۔ دادشاہ اور اس میں شک نہیں ہے کہ علیہ غانیہ وجود خارخی کے لحاظ سے علہ فاعلیہ کی معلول ہوتی ہے تو اس سے معلوم ہوا کہ لام جو داخل في الغرض والقانتہ ہوتا حقیقتاً داخل على المعلول ہوتا ہے ۔ وجهنا نذكر حروفاً اس مقام پر چند حروف
Section: The subsidiary metaphor occurs in particles. The scholars of rhetoric have mentioned that metaphor is of two types: one is original metaphor, which is in generic nouns, and this occurs in generic nouns. The second type is subsidiary metaphor, which occurs in derivatives and particles. By generic noun is meant a solid noun that indicates a generic meaning, and the verbal noun (masdar) from which the derivative is taken. Similarly, the attachments of particle meanings, such as 'min' for the beginning of a limit and 'lam' for causation. So these: beginning and causation, which are among the attachments of particle meanings, are all included under generic nouns. And these meanings of causation or beginning are not particle meanings because they are independent noun meanings. However, the expressions of particle meanings are implicational (iltizami), i.e., the meanings of particles refer to them by a kind of implication. And the second type is subsidiary metaphor; it occurs in derivatives and particles. They called it 'tab'iyya' because it does not occur except as subsidiary to the occurrence of the metaphor in the source of the derivative. That is, first this metaphor occurs in the source (masdar) from which the derivative is taken, then secondarily and subsidiarily in the derivative. The derivative may be a verb or a form of attribute, as you say: 'the state is speaking' i.e., indicating. Here the word 'natiqa' is a metaphor for 'indicating'. This is a subsidiary metaphor by following the metaphor of 'natiq' (speaking) for 'indication'. That is, first a metaphor of 'speaking' for 'indication' occurs, then from this 'speaking' the derivative 'natiqa' is used as a metaphor for 'indicator'. So the metaphor of 'natiqa' is not directly for 'indicator' but through the mediation of the metaphor of the source. Similarly, the metaphor in particles: the metaphor in particles occurs first in the meaning of the particle's attachment, then in the particle itself. For example, 'lam' which is for causation: in its metaphor, first a metaphor of causation is made for 'following' (ta'qib). 'Ta'qib' means coming after. By 'causation' what is intended is 'following' (ta'qib). And this following is general, whether it is the following of the effect after the cause, as in 'the following of the effect for the cause', or other types of following, such as the following of death after birth. Then through the mediation of the metaphor of causation for following, the 'lam' is used as a metaphor for following, as in 'to the death of children and the ruin of buildings' because death occurs after birth and in its wake. So it is as if birth is made a cause for death, so the lam of causation is used intending that death will certainly occur after birth without failure, like the occurrence of the effect after the cause. And similarly in 'and the ruin of buildings' because the desolation and ruin of a house occurs after construction, just as death was after birth. This is based on the fact that 'lam' enters upon the final cause (al-illah al-gha'iyyah), and this is the end purpose of the action. And there is no doubt that the final cause (al-illah al-gha'iyyah) in terms of external existence is the effect of the efficient cause. So it is known that 'lam' which enters upon the purpose and end actually enters upon the effect. And we will mention some letters in this place.
Source pages p. 332

Commentary
This passage defines the two types of isti'ara in Hanafi rhetorical theory. The first is 'asliyya' (original metaphor) which occurs in generic nouns (asma' al-ajnas) such as solid nouns and masdars. The second is 'tab'iyya' (subsidiary metaphor) which occurs in derivatives (mushtaqqat) and particles (huruf). The author explains that tab'iyya is called subsidiary because it depends on a prior metaphor in the source (masdar). For example, in 'al-hal natiqa' (the state is speaking), the metaphor of 'natiqa' for 'indicating' is derived from the metaphor of 'natiq' (speaking) for 'indication'. In particles, the process involves first a metaphor in the meaning of the particle's attachment (like causation) and then in the particle itself (e.g., lam used for ta'qib). The example given is 'li-daw al-mawt wa 'bina' al-kharab' (to the death of children and the ruin of buildings) where lam al-ta'lil is used metaphorically for following (ta'qib), because death follows birth. The author concludes that lam actually enters upon the effect (ma'lul) when used for purpose.

Key Terms
استعارہ اصلیہ (isti'ara asliyya): Original metaphor that occurs in generic nouns (asma' al-ajnas).
استعارہ تبعیہ (isti'ara tab'iyya): Subsidiary metaphor that occurs in derivatives and particles, dependent on a prior metaphor in the source word.
لام التعلیل (lam al-ta'lil): The letter 'lam' used for causation, which can be used metaphorically for following (ta'qib).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What must occur first for a subsidiary metaphor (isti'ara tab'iyya) to take place in a derivative?
A) The derivative must be an original noun.
B) A metaphor must first occur in the source (masdar) from which the derivative is taken.
C) The particle must be used literally.
D) The meaning must be abstract without any sensory reference.

Key Takeaway
Subsidiary metaphor (isti'ara tab'iyya) always depends on a prior metaphor in the source, a principle essential for accurate analysis of derivative and particle expressions in legal texts.

Subsidiary Metaphor in Derivatives and Particles
Explaining subsidiary metaphor and its application

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify subsidiary metaphor in a derivative or particle and explain its derivation chain using Hanafi usul principles.

Core Idea
Subsidiary metaphor occurs first in the source then secondarily in the derivative.

In Hanafi usul, understanding metaphor is crucial for interpreting legal texts. This lesson introduces subsidiary metaphor, which occurs in derivatives and particles. Unlike original metaphor in generic nouns, subsidiary metaphor depends on a prior metaphorical transfer in the source word. We will explore how this chain works with examples like 'al-hal natiqa' and the particle 'lam'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the word as derivative or particle
Determine whether the word in question is a verb, participle, or particle (e.g., 'natiqa' is a feminine active participle; 'lam' is a particle).
Derivatives include verbs and participles; particles include prepositions and conjunctions.
2
Find the source (masdar) or correlate meaning
For derivatives, identify the verbal noun (e.g., 'nutq' for 'natiqa'). For particles, identify the independent nominal meaning that the particle indicates (e.g., 'ta'lil' for 'lam').
The source must be a generic noun (ism jins).
3
Recognize the metaphorical transfer in the source
First, the source is used metaphorically for a new meaning. In 'al-hal natiqa', 'nutq' (speech) is metaphorically used for 'dalala' (indication). In the 'lam' example, 'ta'lil' (explanation) is metaphorically used for 'ta'qib' (following).
This first metaphor is original (in a generic noun) because the source is an ism jins.
4
Confirm the derivative carries the metaphor secondarily
The derivative 'natiqa' then carries the metaphor for 'dalla' (indicating) only because 'nutq' already stands for 'dalala'. Similarly, 'lam' carries the metaphor for 'ta'qib' only because 'ta'lil' was already used for 'ta'qib'.
The derivative does not receive the metaphor directly but through the source.

Source Text

مصدر جو مشتق منہ ہوتی ہے اور اسی طرح معنی حرفی کے متعلقات جیسے من لا بتد ا لغایہ اور لام للتعلیل تو یہ ابتدا اور تعلیل جو معانی حرفیہ کے متعلقات میں یہ سب اسماء اجناس میں درج ہیں اور یہ تعلیل یا ابتدا المشتقات و الحروف اور استعار تبعیہ مستقات اور حروف میں ہوتا ہے ۔ انما قالوا تبعية اس کو تبعیہ اس لئے کہا جاتا ہے کہ لا تقع الابتبعيه وقوعهاني المشتق منه یعنی اولاً یہ استعارہ مشتق منہ یعنی مصدر میں واقع ہوگا پھر ثانیا و تبعا مشتق میں ہوگا۔ مشتق خواہ فعل ہو یا صیغہ صفتہ کا کما تقول الحال ناطقة اى دالہ تو یہاں لفظ ناطقہ کا استعارہ ہے دالہ کے لئے یہ استعارہ تبعیر ہے بتبعية استعارة النطق للدلالة يعني اولاً نطق کا استعارہ ہو گا دلالہ کے لئے پھر اسی نطق سے مشتق ناطقہ کا استعارہ ہو گا برائے دالہ تو ناطقہ کا استعارہ والے کے لئے براہ راست نہیں ہوگا بلکہ بواسطہ استعادة المصدر ہوگا وكذا الاستعارة في الحرون فان الاستعارة في الحروف تقع اولاً اولا معنی حروف کے متعلق میں استعارہ ہوتا ہے ۔ ثنیہ پھر حرف میں کالام لام جو برائے تعلیل ہوتا ہے اس کے استعارہ میں اولا تعلیل کا استعارہ برائے تعقیب کیا جائے گا تعقیب کا معنی ہے عقب میں آتا ہے نیراد بالتعليل التعقیب یعنی اولا تعلیل سے تعقیب کا ارادہ کیا جائے اور یہ تعقیب عام ہے خواہ تعقيب العلة المعلول لفظ معلول مرفوع ہو کر فاعل تعقیب ہے اور العلم مفعول مضاف الیہ ہے یعنی معلول کا علہ کے پیچھے آنا اوغیرہ یا اس کے علاوہ کوئی اور تعقیب ہو جیسے تعقيب الموت للولادة ثم بواسطتعا پھر بواسطہ استعارة التعليل للتعقيب یستعار اللام لام کا استعارہ برائے تعقیب ہوگا نحو لد و الموت وابنو اللخراب چونکہ موت ولادة کے بعد اور عقب میں ہوتی ہے تو جعل كان الولادة تو گویا کہ ولادۃ کو موت کے لئے علہ قرار دیا گیا نا ستعمل لام التعلیان لام تعلیل استعمال کر کے ارادہ کیا گیا کہ موت ولادت کے بعد یقیناً واقع ہوگی بلا تخلف وتوع المعلول جیسے معلول کا وقوع بعد از علت قطعی ہوتا ہے ایسے وابنو اللخراب میں کیونکہ کسی دار کا ویران اور خراب ہونا بعد از بنا ہوتا ہے جیسے موت بعد از ولادت تھی رحن ابناء على ان اللام اور یہ اس پر مبنی ہے کہ لام علہ غانیہ [؟] پر داخل ہوتا اور یہی غایتہ غرض من الفعل ہوتی ہے ۔ دادشاہ [؟] اور اس میں شک نہیں ہے کہ علیہ غانیہ وجود خارخی کے لحاظ سے علہ فاعلیہ کی معلول ہوتی ہے تو اس سے معلوم ہوا کہ لام جو داخل في الغرض والقانتہ ہوتا حقیقتاً داخل على المعلول ہوتا ہے ۔
One type is original metaphor, and it occurs in generic nouns (asma' al-ajnas). And the second type is subsidiary metaphor, and it occurs in derivatives and particles. By generic noun is meant a solid noun that signifies generic meaning, and the verbal noun (masdar) from which the derivative is derived, and likewise the correlates of particle meanings, like 'min' for beginning of the limit, and 'lam' for explanation. So these (beginning and explanation) which are among the correlates of particle meanings, all are included in generic nouns. And these (explanation or beginning) are not particle meanings because they are independent nominal meanings. However, the expression of particle meanings is implication, i.e., the meanings of particles refer to them with a kind of implication. And the second type of metaphor is subsidiary, occurring in derivatives and particles. They called it subsidiary because it occurs only secondarily to its occurrence in the source (mashaqq minhu). That is, first the metaphor occurs in the source, i.e., the verbal noun, then secondarily and subsequently in the derivative. The derivative may be a verb or a form of an attribute. As you say 'al-hal natiga' i.e., 'indicating' – here the word 'natiga' is a metaphor for 'dalla' (indicating). This is a subsidiary metaphor, following the metaphor of 'nutq' for 'dalala'. That is, first 'nutq' is used metaphorically for 'dalala', then from that 'nutq' the derivative 'natiga' is used metaphorically for 'dalla'. So 'natiga' is not directly metaphorical for 'dalle' but through the mediation of the metaphor of the verbal noun. Similarly, metaphor in particles: metaphor in particles occurs first in the correlate of the particle meaning, then in the particle itself. For example, the lam that is for explanation. In its metaphor, first, explanation is used metaphorically for 'ta'qib' (following). 'Ta'qib' means 'comes after'. So by explanation is intended following. And this following is general, whether it be the following of the effect after the cause – the word 'ma'lul' is raised as the subject of 'ta'qib' and 'al-illa' is the object of the genitive – i.e., the effect coming after the cause, etc., or any other following, like the following of death after birth. Then through the mediation of this metaphor of explanation for following, the lam is used metaphorically for following, as in 'li-daw al-mawt wa binu al-kharab'. Since death occurs after and following birth, it is as if birth is made a cause for death, so the lam of explanation is used, intending that death will certainly occur after birth without delay like the occurrence of the effect after the cause. Similarly in 'wa binu al-kharab'. And this is based on the fact that the lam enters onto the goal/purpose and this is the intended end of the action. And there is no doubt that the goal/purpose, in terms of external existence, is the effect of the efficient cause. So it becomes clear that the lam which enters onto the purpose and end actually enters onto the effect.
Source pages p. 332

Commentary
This passage is a commentary within Hanafi usul al-fiqh explaining the concept of subsidiary metaphor (isti'ara tabi'iyya). The author distinguishes two types of metaphor: original, which occurs in generic nouns (asma' al-ajnas), and subsidiary, which occurs in derivatives and particles. Subsidiary metaphor is called such because it does not occur directly but after the metaphor in the source (masdar). For example, in 'al-hal natiga' (the state speaks), the word 'natiga' is a derivative of 'nutq' (speech). Here, first 'nutq' is used metaphorically to mean 'indication', then 'natiga' is used metaphorically as a derivative. Similarly, in particles like 'lam' for explanation, the metaphor first occurs in the meaning of explanation (ta'lil) to signify following (ta'qib), and then the particle 'lam' is used for that meaning. The example given is 'li-daw al-mawt' (for the inevitability of death) and 'wa binu al-kharab' (and building for ruin), where the lam indicates following rather than cause. The commentator emphasizes that this metaphorical usage is based on the concept that the goal (ghaya) is an effect of the cause.

Key Terms
استعارہ تبعیہ (isti'ara tabi'iyya): Subsidiary metaphor that occurs in derivatives and particles, first in the source (masdar) then in the derivative.
مشتقات (mushtaqqat): Derived words such as verbs and participles, which are derived from a verbal noun (masdar).
استعارہ اصلیہ (isti'ara asliyya): Original metaphor that occurs directly in generic nouns (asma' al-ajnas), such as the verbal noun itself.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the phrase 'al-hal natiqa', how is 'natiqa' a subsidiary metaphor?
A) Directly from 'natiqa' to 'dalla'.
B) First 'nutq' is metaphor for 'dalala', then 'natiqa' for 'dalla'.
C) Both 'nutq' and 'natiqa' are used literally.
D) 'Natiqa' is a generic noun.

Key Takeaway
Subsidiary metaphor in derivatives and particles depends on a prior metaphor in the source (generic noun), making the derivative's meaning secondary.

Conditional Divorce with Repetition: Simultaneous Occurrence
Ruling on multiple divorces with repeated condition or multiplied consequences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that multiple conditional sentences with the same condition trigger all consequences simultaneously upon fulfillment, and distinguish the effect of repeating the condition versus multiplying consequences with conjunction.

Core Idea
Repeated conditional sentences with the same condition cause all consequences to occur together upon fulfillment.

This lesson examines a key principle in Hanafi usul regarding ta'liq (conditional divorce). When a husband repeats the same conditional statement multiple times, each repetition is an independent ta'liq, but because the condition is identical, all divorces are triggered at once when the condition is fulfilled. The order of words—whether condition precedes consequence or vice versa—does not affect this outcome. The passage also demonstrates that when consequences are multiplied with conjunction (waw) and placed before the condition, all are suspended on the same condition and occur together, as agreed upon in the madhhab.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage presents two cases: (1) repetition of the full conditional sentence (condition + consequence repeated three times) and (2) a single conditional sentence with multiple consequences linked by 'wa' placed before the condition.
2
Apply the principle of ta'liq
In Hanafi usul, the suspension (ta'liq) attaches to the condition at the time of condition fulfillment, not at the time of utterance. Therefore, all separate ta'liqs are triggered simultaneously if the condition is the same.
The repetition and order exist only at the time of speech, not at fulfillment.
3
Compare with reversed order
The passage notes that if the consequences are placed before the condition, the ruling remains the same: all are suspended on the delayed condition and occur together upon fulfillment. This is agreed upon (ittifaqan) in the madhhab.
The order does not affect the ta'liq because the condition is the same; the suspension is determined by the meaning, not arrangement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ترتیب نہیں ہے ۔ لہذا جو بوقت تكلم مرتب تھیں بوقت تحقق شرط تمام مجتمع ہو کر واقع ہوں گی یہ ایسے ہے کیا اذاکر ثلث صرات الخ یہ ایسے ہے جیسے غیر مدخولہ کے لئے اپنے قول ان دخلت الدار فانت طالق کو ثلث مرات متکرر کرتا مثلاً یوں کہتا ان دخلت الدار فانت طالق ان دخلت الدار فانت طالق ان دخلت الدار فانت طالق تو بوقت تحقق شرط یعنی دخول دار تینوں طلاق واقع ہو جائیں ۔ کیونکہ یہ تکرار و ترتیب بوقت تکم تھی نه بوقت تحقق شرط كذا ههنا تو اسی طرح تین طلاق واقع ہوں گی اس قول مذکور میں جہاں بلا تکرار شرط مع الاجزية المتعدده کہا ان دخلت الدار فانت طالق وطالق وطالق مان قدم الاجزية اگر جزائیں بصورت تعدد مقدم کر دے اور شرط مؤخر مثلاً یوں کہہ غیر مدخولہ کو انت طالق وطالق وطالق ان دخلت الدار يقع المثلث اتفاقا کیونکہ اجزہ یہ جو ابتدا متوقفہ تھیں وہ تمام کی تمام دفعہ اس شرط مؤخر کے ساتھ معلق ہو جائینگی اور بوقت تحقق شرط تمام واقع ہو جائینگی
Order is not [established]. Therefore, those [divorces] that were ordered at the time of speech, at the time of fulfillment of condition they will all occur simultaneously. This is like when he says to a non-consummated wife, 'If you enter the house, you are divorcees,' repeating it three times – for example, he says, 'If you enter the house, you are divorced; if you enter the house, you are divorced; if you enter the house, you are divorced' – then at the time of fulfillment of condition, i.e., entering the house, all three divorces occur. Because this repetition and order was at the time of speech, not at the time of fulfillment of condition. Similarly here, thus three divorces will occur. In this mentioned statement where without repetition of the condition he says with multiple consequences: 'If you enter the house, you are divorced and divorced and divorced' – if he presents the consequences first in the form of multiplicity and delays the condition, for example, saying to a non-consummated wife, 'You are divorced and divorced and divorced, if you enter the house' – [then] the three [divorces] occur by agreement because these consequences which were initially dependent [on the condition] all become suspended with the delayed condition and at the time of fulfillment of condition all occur.
Source pages p. 335

Commentary
This passage explains the Hanafi principle governing conditional divorces with multiple pronouncements. When a husband repeats the conditional sentence, each repetition is a separate ta'liq, but since the condition is identical, all divorces are triggered simultaneously upon fulfillment. The order of utterance does not create a sequence in occurrence. Similarly, when the husband uses conjunction (waw) to multiply the consequences and places them before the condition, all are suspended on the same condition and occur together. This is the agreed position (ittifaqan) in the madhhab. The key usul rule is that the ta'liq attaches at the time of condition fulfillment, not utterance.

Key Terms
تعليق (ta'liq): Suspending a legal consequence upon the occurrence of a future condition.
جزاء (jaza): The legal consequence (e.g., divorce) that results when the condition is fulfilled.
شرط (shart): A future event whose occurrence triggers the legal consequence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband says to his non-consummated wife, 'If you enter the house, you are divorced, and if you enter the house, you are divorced, and if you enter the house, you are divorced' (three separate conditional sentences), how many divorces occur upon her entry?
A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) None

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, when a condition is repeated in independent conditional statements, all consequences are suspended on the same condition and occur simultaneously upon fulfillment.

Interpreting Waw in Emancipation: Sequential vs. Simultaneous Freedom
Ruling on marriage validity after emancipation using conjunctions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that waw can imply order (tartib) when a legal qarina is present, and derive the ruling for the marriage of two slave women under different emancipation methods.

Core Idea
When the master frees two slave women using 'waw', Hanafis treat it as sequential—only the first marriage becomes valid.

This lesson examines a classic Hanafi usul problem: does the conjunction waw (و) in a master's statement freeing two slave women imply simultaneity or order? The answer determines the validity of the women's marriages to a free man who married them without permission. By analyzing the textual operation and the legal qarina (prohibition of marrying a slave woman on a free woman), we see how Hanafi jurists derive a sequential interpretation to avoid contradiction with established fiqh rules.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal scenarios
Recognize the three ways a master can free two slave women: simultaneously, in separate statements, or with a conjunction (waw) in one statement. The passage gives a ruling for each.
Simultaneous: both valid; separate: only first valid; conjunction: only first valid (Hanafi view).
2
Pinpoint the usul issue
The dispute centers on the interpretation of waw in the third scenario. Does it indicate simultaneity or order? The Hanafi position is that it indicates order due to a legal qarina.
The qarina is the prohibition of having a slave wife while already married to a free woman.
3
Analyze the textual category
Classify the master's statement 'I free this one and this one' in terms of usul categories. The waw itself is a grammatical tool; the statement becomes ambiguous (mujmal) unless a qarina clarifies it.
Mujmal requires bayan; here the qarina provides the bayan for tartib.
4
Apply the Hanafi principle
Using the principle that a mujmal text is interpreted by a legal qarina, derive that the first mentioned slave is freed first, making her marriage valid; the second remains a slave, making her marriage invalid.
This preserves the rule: a free man cannot marry a slave woman if he already has a free wife.
5
Note the presentation difference
Observe that Shams al-A'imma and Fakhr al-Islam present the issue differently (one contract vs. two), but the ruling remains consistent. This shows that usul principles yield the same result regardless of procedural framing.
Details in al-Jami' al-Kabir and al-Talwih.

Summary: Question: When a person marries two slave women without permission of their master – then the master frees both slave women simultaneously, e.g., he says: 'I free both of you' – then the marriage of both becomes valid. And if he frees them with two separate statements, e.g., he says: 'I free this one', then after a while he says: 'I free this one' – then in this case the marriage of the first is valid and the marriage of the second is invalid, because in the second there is marrying a slave woman upon a free woman, which is not valid, since the first marriage became valid as soon as she became free, and the second at that time is a slave woman, so this becomes invalid. Or if he does not free with two separate statements but uses a conjunction (harf 'atf) in one statement, saying: 'I free this one and this one' – then the marriage of the second is invalid. In this case of conjunction, the marriage of the first freed woman is valid and the second is invalid. The intended question is this case of conjunction, because here [the word] waw is used, and they have made it for order (tartib), because the first reference (participial) freed first, so her marriage became valid, and at that moment the second is not freed. So the marriage of a slave woman upon a free woman is not valid. This is the way the mas'ala is presented by Shams al-A'imma. As for Fakhr al-Islam, his presentation differs. He says: A person marries two slave women as a guardian (fuduli) to another person without permission of the masters and without permission of the husband. His saying 'without permission of the husband' is unnecessary to specify. On the assumption that this specification is made, then it is necessary that another guardian on behalf of the husband accept this marriage, because one guardian cannot act as guardian for both parties in marriage. And it has been recorded in the footnotes of Fakhr al-Islam that this condition was added that the marriage of the two slave women be in one contract (bi-'aqd wahid), following the presentation in al-Jami' al-Kabir. Although it is not necessary, because our discussion does not differ due to one contract or multiple contracts. And in al-Jami' al-Kabir, the issue is restricted to one contract because many issues are organized in one thread, some of which have differing rulings based on one contract or two contracts, e.g., the marriage of two slave women with their consent and the consent of their master. But consent is not [present], so this issue has different ruling based on one or two contracts; for that purpose the restriction to one contract was made. Details are in al-Jami' al-Kabir, and some detail in al-Talwih.
Source pages p. 335

Commentary
This passage presents a detailed Hanafi ruling on the validity of marrying two slave women without their master's permission. The marriage is suspended on the master's ratification or emancipation. If the master frees both simultaneously, both marriages become valid. If he frees them separately, only the first becomes valid because the first woman becomes free and her marriage is valid; the second remains a slave at that moment, and marrying a slave woman while already married to a free woman (the first) is invalid. When the master uses the conjunction 'waw' to free both in one statement, Hanafis interpret the waw as implying order (tartib) in this context, so the first mentioned is freed first, making her marriage valid and the second's marriage invalid. The commentator also notes a difference in how Shams al-A'imma and Fakhr al-Islam present the issue, particularly regarding whether the marriage is done in one contract or two, but concludes the ruling is the same.

Key Terms
عتق (itq / 'itq): Emancipation of a slave, making the slave free.
فضولي (fuduli): A person who acts on behalf of another without authorization.
عطف ('atf): Grammatical conjunction, especially with waw.
قرينة (qarina): A contextual indicator that determines the intended meaning of an ambiguous expression.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The waw in emancipation of two slave women is interpreted as sequential to uphold the rule against having a slave wife alongside a free wife.

Emancipation Through Confirmation in Terminal Illness: Conditions and Rulings
Ruling on the effect of a son's confirmation of his father's slave emancipation in terminal illness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi conditions for a son's confirmation (iqrar) of his father's slave emancipations during marad al-mawt to determine the share of freedom for each slave.

Core Idea
A son's confirmation frees only one-third of each slave if no other heir, no other property, equal values, and consecutive affirmation.

This passage delves into a nuanced scenario where a son reports that his father, in his terminal illness, freed three slaves. The ruling pivots on several conditions: the absence of other heirs, lack of other property, equal slave values, and the son's consecutive confirmation. Understanding these conditions is key to applying the correct share of freedom and the requirement of sa'ayah (earning) for the remainder.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Subject
Pinpoint the legal act: the father's emancipation of three slaves during marad al-mawt, and the son's confirmation (iqrar) of that act.
The son's iqrar is not a bequest but an admission that affects his own inheritance share.
2
Analyze the First Condition: No Other Heir
Confirm that the son is the sole heir. If another heir existed, the iqrar would only bind the son's share, and the other heirs' shares would require sa'ayah from the slaves.
The passage states: 'if the deceased had another son... the ruling would apply only to the share of the confirmer.'
3
Analyze the Second Condition: No Other Property
Assume the deceased has no property besides the slaves. If other property existed and the slaves' value were within one-third of the estate, they would all be freed fully.
This condition ensures the emancipation does not exceed the one-third bequest limit.
4
Examine the Condition of Equal Value and Consecutive Affirmation
The slaves must be of equal value. The son's confirmation must be consecutive (muttasilan) without pauses. If consecutive, one-third of each slave becomes free.
The text warns of a different ruling if there is a pause (muskit) between affirmations.
5
Derive the Final Ruling
Combine all conditions: with sole heir, no other property, equal values, and consecutive confirmation, each slave is one-third free. The remaining two-thirds require the slaves to earn through sa'ayah.
This ruling respects both the deceased's intent and the legal limits on bequests.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وان قال اعتق الي في مرض موته الا اگر ایک شخص کہتا ہے کہ میرے باپ نے اپنی مرض الموت میں اعتق هذا وندا و ترا یعنی تین غلاموں کے متعلق کہتا ہے کہ اس کو اور اس کو اور اس کو ازاد کیا تھا۔ ولا وارث ولا مال له سوئی ذلت اور اس کا وارث بجز اس ابن مقر کے کوئی نہیں ہے کیونکہ اس کے علاوہ اگر اس میت کا اور ابن بھی ہو تو پھر حکم عشق صرف اس مقر کے حصہ میں نافذ ہوگا اور باقی میں سعایتہ واجب ہوگی اور اس میت کا مال ان تین غلاموں کے علاوہ اور کچھ نہیں ہے کیونکہ ان کے علاوہ اور مال بھی ہو اور یہ غلام اس کی ثلث میں آسکتے ہیں تو تمام ازاد ہو جائیں گے اس لئے یہ دونوں قیدیں ضروری ہیں اور یہ قید بھی معتبر ہے کہ قیمت ان کی آپس میں برابر ہے خان اقر متصلا اگر ان تینوں کے متعلق یہ اقرار متصلا کرتا ہے تو ہر غلام کی ثلث ازاد ہوگی دان مسكت فيما بين ذلك
And if he says: 'My father freed [slaves] during his terminal illness' – i.e., a person says: 'My father in his terminal illness freed this, that, and that' – meaning three slaves, he says: 'He freed this one, and this one, and this one.' And there is no heir and no property except that [son], meaning his only heir is this confirming son, because if the deceased had another son besides him, then the ruling of the declaration would apply only to the share of the confirmer, and in the remainder the slaves would have to work (sa'ayah) to earn their freedom. And the deceased's property is nothing except these three slaves, because if there were other property and these slaves are within one-third, they would all be freed. Therefore both conditions are necessary. And this condition is also valid: that their values are equal. If he makes this confirmation consecutively (muttasilan), then one-third of each slave becomes free. But if he pauses in between [the affirmations]...
Source pages p. 336

Commentary
This passage discusses a scenario where a person reports that his father, during a terminal illness, declared the emancipation of three slaves. The rulings hinge on several conditions: (1) The only heir is the son who is reporting (the 'muqirr'). If there were another son, the freedom would apply only to the share of the reporting son, and the other slaves would need to pay off their value (sa'ayah). (2) The deceased has no other property besides these slaves. If there were other property and the slaves' value is within the one-third of the estate allowed for bequests, they would all be freed. (3) The slaves are equal in value. The commentator then says that if the son makes the confirmation consecutively (without interruption), one-third of each slave becomes free. The sentence ends indicating a discussion on what happens if there is a pause (muskit) between affirmations, but the text cuts off.

Key Terms
مرض الموت (marad al-mawt): Illness from which death is likely, during which bequests and emancipations are limited to one-third of the estate.
سعاية (sa'ayah): A slave's earning of money to pay for their freedom when partially emancipated.
إقرار (iqrar): A confession or admission that creates legal obligations, in this context about the father's acts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man says during terminal illness: 'I freed this slave, and this slave, and this slave' (three slaves). The only heir is his son who confirms this. There is no other property. The slaves are of equal value. What part of each slave becomes free if the son confirms consecutively?
A) The entire slave
B) One-third of each
C) One-half of each
D) None, because it exceeds the one-third limit

Key Takeaway
A son's consecutive confirmation of his father's slave emancipations during terminal illness, with no other heir or property and equal slave values, frees one-third of each slave, with the remainder requiring sa'ayah.

The Rule of Waw Between Sentences: Sharing of Predicate
Explains when waw between sentences causes sharing of predicate

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule regarding the conjunction waw between sentences to determine whether sharing of a predicate occurs, and identify the practical difference between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn in the case of conditional divorce.

Core Idea
When waw joins independent sentences, each stands alone; sharing only occurs when the second sentence is incomplete without the first.

In Arabic, the conjunction waw can join two sentences. The general principle in Hanafi usul is that waw between two sentences does not cause them to share a ruling; each sentence has its own independent meaning. However, an exception occurs when the second sentence is dependent on the first for completion—i.e., when the second needs the same predicate or condition (mutammim) as the first. This lesson explores that rule, its application in divorce law, and the intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish independent vs dependent sentences
Identify whether the second sentence can stand alone or requires something from the first to complete its meaning. If independent, no sharing; if dependent, sharing may occur.
An independent sentence has its own full subject and predicate.
2
Identify when the second sentence needs the first
Look for cases where the second part is attached to a predicate of a subject or apodosis of a condition, and the same mutammim (completer) applies. Example: 'If you enter, you are divorced, and divorced, and divorced'.
The mutammim here is the condition 'if you enter'.
3
Apply to the conditional divorce example: Imam vs Sahibayn
Imam holds the single condition applies to all consequents; Sahibayn estimate separate conditions. Work through the reasoning: Imam says the second and third 'divorced' are لغو; Sahibayn say three divorces occur.
The practical difference: number of divorces.
4
Examine exceptions where union is impossible
When the mutammim cannot be the same (e.g., 'Zayd came and Amr'), the second requires its own estimated predicate. This prevents sharing.
The action 'came' is different for each subject.
5
Consider extension to Quranic verses
Some scholars apply this rule to Quranic conjunctive sentences like 'Establish prayer and give zakat', arguing that the obligation is shared, so minors exempt from zakat if not obligated to pray.
This is presented as a plausible view, not the adopted Hanafi position.

واتوا الزکواۃ کا چونکہ صبی اقیموا الصلوۃ کا مخاطب
And the waw of conjunction sometimes enters between two sentences, and then it does not necessitate sharing. At that time, it will not be a cause of sharing; rather it will only give the benefit of combining the two sentences in meaning. Each sentence independently benefits its own meaning. For example, in his saying 'This one is divorced by three, and this one is divorced,' in that case the second woman is divorced by a single divorce, while the first is divorced by three divorces. The second will not share in the three divorces. Each sentence obtains its own ruling separately. And this sharing is obligatory only when the last part needs the first. That is, when the last statement needs the first, then it will share with the first in that part with which the first was completed. This generally occurs when conjunction is made by waw onto the predicate of a subject or onto the apodosis of a condition. The same [completer] that is for the first will be for the second, without estimating something like it, provided that union of the completer is not prevented. For example, 'If you enter the house, you are divorced, and divorced, and divorced' – the latter two conjoined words are in need of the first, and for them the same completer 'If you enter' that is mentioned in the first [suffices]. It is not necessary to estimate a separate 'If you enter' with each 'divorced'. This is unlike repeating his saying 'If you enter, you are divorced' – like this saying with the condition repeated. It is not like this, so three divorces do not occur according to Imam [Abu Hanifa] in the case of an unconsummated wife. Rather, she will become irrevocably divorced with the first, and the second and third are لغو (void). Because it is possible [to attach them to the same condition], it is possible that these multiple parts are attached to the single same condition mentioned first, 'If you enter the house'. Here, one does not need to estimate a similar [condition]. That is, a separate condition will not be estimated for each. So much so that it is not to be set as 'If you enter, you are divorced; if you enter, you are divorced; if you enter, you are divorced' as according to the Sahibayn. According to Imam, it will not be so. Or if estimation is made – that is, the same single completer is not sufficient, but a similar one must be estimated – that will be when union of the completer is prevented. This is like when he said 'If union of the completer is prevented' as in 'Zayd came and Amr' because the coming of Zayd is different from the coming of Amr; therefore, in this place, one estimates 'and Amr came'. And some have made sharing obligatory in conjunction of sentences as well. They even said that by means of [؟] the Qur'an and its correspondence in arrangement indicates correspondence in ruling. So they said: 'Establish prayer and give zakat' – here conjunction of sentences necessitates correspondence in ruling. Since prayer is not obligatory upon a minor, therefore zakat is also not obligatory upon a minor. According to them, both sentences share in the ruling. This also seems plausible that according to them this issue is based on the fact that the addressee of 'Establish prayer' is the same addressee of 'And give zakat'. Since the minor is not addressed by 'Establish prayer'... [text cuts off]
Source pages p. 339

Commentary
This passage discusses the rule of the conjunctive waw when it comes between two sentences. The general principle is that waw between sentences does not normally cause the second sentence to share in the specific ruling of the first; each sentence is independent. For example, if a man says to his wife 'You are divorced by three, and you are divorced' (so two statements), the first divorce is triple, the second is single; the second wife does not get the triple. However, sharing does occur when the second part is incomplete without the first, i.e., when the latter part needs the former for completion. This happens typically when the conjunction attaches to the predicate of a subject or to the apodosis of a condition. In such cases, the same completer (e.g., the condition) applies to all conjoined parts without needing to be repeated. The classic example is: 'If you enter the house, you are divorced, and divorced, and divorced.' According to Imam Abu Hanifa, this single condition ('if you enter') applies to all three 'divorced' words, so only one divorce occurs (or at most one set) – the first triggers the divorce, and the rest are لغو (void) because they cannot be repeated without a new condition. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that each 'divorced' is a separate conditional sentence with an estimated repeated condition, resulting in three divorces. The text then addresses when the same completer is not sufficient, namely when the union is impossible, as in 'Zayd came and Amr' – the action is different, so one estimates 'and Amr came'. Finally, some scholars extend the principle to conjunction of sentences in general, arguing that the Quranic verse 'اقيموا الصلاة واتوا الزكاة' implies that the obligation of zakat is upon the same people as prayer, so minors are exempt from both. This is presented as a plausible view but not necessarily the adopted one in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
مشاركة (mushāraka): Sharing: the second conjunct sharing the same predicate or ruling as the first.
متم (mutammim): Completer: the part that completes the meaning (e.g., the condition 'if you enter' or the predicate 'came').
لغو (laghw): Void, ineffective: a statement that has no legal effect, as when a divorce is uttered after the wife is already divorced irrevocably.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, if a man says to his wife 'If you enter the house, you are divorced, and divorced, and divorced' and she has not been consummated, how many divorces occur?
A) Three divorces
B) One divorce (ba'in)
C) Two divorces
D) No divorce because of ambiguity

Key Takeaway
The rule of waw between sentences: sharing occurs only when the second sentence is dependent on the first for completion; otherwise, each sentence stands alone.

Conjunction of Conditional Sentences with Waw: The Principle of Shirkah
Hanafi usul rule on conjunction of conditional sentences with waw

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul rule of عطف (conjunction) with واو in conditional sentences to determine whether a second clause shares in the condition or stands independently.

Core Idea
Conjunction with waw defaults to sharing in the result unless the conjoined clause lacks the strength of a singular.

Understanding how conditional sentences function is vital in Hanafi fiqh, especially in areas like divorce (talāq) and manumission (itq). The particle واو can either join the second clause to the entire condition-result or only to the result. This lesson unpacks the criteria that determine which relationship holds.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default rule of 'atf
Recall that the default function of the conjunction waw is to indicate sharing (shirkah) between the conjoined parts. In conditional sentences, this means the second clause should normally be conjoined to the result (jaza') rather than the entire condition-result compound.
The principle: al-waw li al-'atf, wa al-'atf yufidu al-shirkah.
2
Understand the concept of quwwat mufrad
A sentence that is treated like a singular (mufrad) in terms of dependency—it needs the preceding part to be meaningful. In this context, a nominal sentence (jumlah ismiyyah) that resembles the result in form has quwwat mufrad and is therefore suitable for conjunction to the result.
The result (jaza') of a conditional is usually a nominal sentence, so a conjoined nominal sentence is naturally suitable.
3
Analyze the suitability (munasaba) between conjoined parts
When the second clause is a nominal sentence and the result is also nominal, there is a strong suitability. This preponderates that the conjunction is only on the result, preserving the principle of shirkah.
If conjoined to the total, the suitability between the second clause and the result would disappear.
4
Distinguish between dependent and independent clauses
If the conjoined sentence lacks quwwat mufrad (e.g., a verbal sentence or a complete sentence that does not need the preceding), it is independent. The waw then indicates mere sequence (nasaq), not sharing.
Example: 'اقيموا الصلاة وآتوا الزكاة' – the waw is only for sequence, not sharing.
5
Apply to the given example
In 'إن دخلت الدار فأنت طالق ومَرْتَك طالق', the explicit mention of 'طالق' with 'ومرتك' indicates that the conjunction is to the total (condition+result), because 'ومرتك' alone would have sufficed if it were only to the result. Thus, 'ومرتك طالق' is not conditional.
Contrast with 'وعندي حر' where the conjunction is to the result, making the freeing conditional.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بانعاني قوة المفرد تو اس کا جواب دیا کہ باوجود جملہ نامہ ہونے کے یہ سمنزلہ مفرد کے ہے کہ محتاج الی الاولی ہے کیونکہ اس کو جزا کے ساتھ جملہ اسمیہ ہونے میلی مناسبت حاصل ہے لہذا یہ امر مرجح ہے کہ اس کا عطف صرف جزا پر ہوتا کہ عطف اسمیہ علی الا سمیہ ہو لا علی مجموع الشرط والجن اکیونکہ اس صورت میں معطوفین کے مابین مناسبت مذکورہ ختم ہو جائے گی۔ فاذا کانت جب یہ معطوف على المجزا ہوا تو یہ قوت مفرد میں ہوگا ۔ لان جزاء الشرط کیونکہ جزائے شرط حملہ کا بعض حصہ ہوتا ہے جملہ نامہ نہیں ہوتا وايضا الواو للعطف اور یہ دوسری وجہ ہے حاصل یہ کہ واو عطف کے لئے ہے اور عطف میں شرکۃ اصل ہے فتحصل على الشركة لہذا جہاں تک ممکن ہو اس اصل کی رعایت کرتے ہوئے واد کو شرکتہ پر محمول کیا جائے گا۔ وھذا اذا کان المعطوف اور یہ اس وقت ہے جب کہ معطوف اپنے ماقبل کی طرف حقیقتاً محتاج ہو۔ کمانی المفرد جیسے کہ معطوف مفرد ہو۔ ارحکما یا معطوف اپنے ماقبال کی طرف حکما محتاج ہو یہ اس جملہ میں ہوتا ہے جس کو قوۃ مفرد میں اعتبار کرنا ممکن ہو اس وقت محمول علی الشرکۃ ہو گا تاکہ واو عطف اپنے اصل پر بقدر امکان جاری رہے اما اذالم ممکن جب شرکتہ پر محمول کرنا ممکن نہ ہو تو فلا تحمل پھر محمول علی الشرکتہ نہیں کیا جائیگا وهذا اذا كان المعطوف جملة لا تكون في قوة المفرد سياس وقت ہوگا جب معطوف ایسا جملہ ہو جو قوۃ مفرد میں نہیں ہے تو یہ اپنے ماقبل کی طرف اصلاً محتاج نہیں ہو گا کسانی اقيموا الصلاة والوا الزکوۃ میں تو یہاں و او صرف نسق و ترتیب کے لئے ہوگی شرکت نہیں ہو گی ففی قولہ ان دخلت الدار فانت طالق ومرتك طالق میں وضر تک طالق کو دونوں وجہ پر محمول کرنا ممکن تو ہے لكن اظهار الخبر ليكن لفظ و ممر تک کے ساتھ لفظ طالق کو بصورت خبر ظاہر کرنا اس کو ترجیح دیتا ہے کہ اس کا عطف مجموعی شرط و جزا پر ہو نہ صرف جزا پر لانه لوکان معطونا کیونکہ اگر یہ صرف جزا پر مطوف ہوتا تو و مر تک کہنا کافی تھا ساتھ لفظ طالق کے کہنے کی ضرورت نہ تھی تو و عیدی حرء میں عتق غلام متعلق بالشرط ہوگا بخلاف وضر تک طالق یہ متعلق بالشرط نہیں ہو گا
Regarding the meaning of the strength of the singular, the answer is given that despite being a complete sentence, it is considered like a singular because it needs the first part. Since it has suitability with the result (jaza) in being a nominal sentence, therefore this is a preponderant factor that its conjunction is only on the result so that the conjunction is of nominal on nominal, not on the total of condition and result, because in that case the mentioned suitability between the conjoined parts would disappear. So when it is conjoined to the result, it will be in the strength of a singular, because the result of the condition is a part of the sentence, not a complete sentence. And also, the waw is for conjunction, and in conjunction, sharing (shirkah) is the original principle. Therefore, wherever possible, maintaining this principle, the waw will be applied to sharing. And this is when the conjoined item is actually dependent on what precedes it, like a singular, or when the conjoined item is legally dependent, which occurs in a sentence that can be considered as having the strength of a singular. Then it will be taken as sharing so that the waw of conjunction remains on its principle as much as possible. But if it is not possible to take it as sharing, then it will not be taken as sharing. And this is when the conjoined item is a sentence that is not in the strength of a singular; then it will not be dependent on its preceding at all, like in 'aqimu al-salat wa-atu al-zakat' where the waw is only for sequence (nasaq) and order, not sharing. So in his statement 'in dakhalti al-dar fa-anti taliq wa-murtaka taliq', the phrase 'wa-murtaka taliq' can be applied in both ways, but the expression of the news by manifesting the word 'taliq' with 'wa-murtaka' gives preponderance that its conjunction is on the total of condition and result, not only on the result, because if it were conjoined only to the result, it would have been sufficient to say 'wa-murtaka' without the need to mention 'taliq'. So in 'wa-'indi hurr', the freeing of the slave is dependent on the condition, contrary to 'wa-murtaka taliq' which is not dependent on the condition.
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul rule about the conjunction (عطف) of conditional sentences using the particle واو. The author argues that when a second conditional clause is introduced by واو, it should normally be conjoined to the result (جزاء) rather than the whole condition-result compound, because the second clause, being a nominal sentence, has a natural suitability (مناسبت) with the result, which is also a nominal sentence. This preference maintains the principle of sharing (شركة) inherent in the conjunction. However, if the conjoined item is a sentence that lacks the strength of a singular (قوة المفرد), it must be independent and the واو is merely for sequence. The author illustrates with the example 'إن دخلت الدار فأنت طالق و مَرْتَك طالق' (If you enter the house, you are divorced, and your servant is divorced). Here, the explicit mentioning of 'طالق' with 'ومرتك' (wa-murtaka) indicates that it is conjoined to the whole conditional, not just the result, because otherwise 'ومرتك' alone would suffice. In contrast, in 'وعندي حر' (and I have a free slave), the freeing is dependent on the condition. This analysis is crucial for understanding how conditional sentences operate in Hanafi usul, particularly in matters of divorce and manumission.

Key Terms
عطف ('atf): Conjunction of a sentence or word to another using a particle like واو.
شرکۃ (shirkah): Sharing in the grammatical or legal effect of the conjoined phrase.
قوۃ مفرد (quwwat mufrad): Strength of a singular; a phrase that functions like a single word in dependency.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example 'إن دخلت الدار فأنت طالق ومَرْتَك طالق', why does the explicit mention of 'طالق' indicate conjunction to the whole conditional?
A) Because 'طالق' is part of the result.
B) Because without it, the phrase would be incomplete.
C) Because 'ومرتك' alone would have sufficed if it were only conjoined to the result.
D) Because the واو always conjoins to the total.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, conjunction of a second conditional clause with waw defaults to sharing in the result, but explicit wording or lack of quwwat mufrad can make it independent.

The Particle 'Fa' for Immediate Succession in Usul al-Fiqh
Usul rule on the particle fa for ta'qib and its entry on the effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule of 'fa' for ta'qib to interpret conditional and causal constructions in Hanafi legal texts, including identifying exceptions.

Core Idea
'Fa' signals immediate consequence: the effect directly follows the cause; entering on the cause is exceptional.

In Arabic, the particle 'fa' is a key tool for legal interpretation. In Hanafi usul, it primarily indicates ta'qib—immediate succession. This passage from Al-Talweeh explores how 'fa' functions on results and effects, with legal examples from sales, manumission, and contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the particle and its primary meaning
The passage begins by stating 'al-fa' li al-ta'qib', meaning 'fa' is for immediate succession. Understand that the thing after 'fa' occurs immediately after what precedes it without delay.
2
Distinguish between entry on result (jaza) and effect (ma'lul)
The author explains that 'fa' enters on the result in conditional sentences (e.g., 'in dakhalti... fa-anti taliq') and on the effect in causal sequences (e.g., 'ja'a al-shita' fa-tajjab').
Effect (ma'lul) is the consequence of a cause ('illa).
3
Analyze examples where cause and effect are identical in existence but different in concept
The passage gives examples like 'saqa fa-arwa' and freeing a parent slave. Here, the cause and effect are the same act but differ in conceptual meaning.
This shows linguistic nuance: 'fa' does not necessarily indicate two separate events; it can link two aspects of the same event.
4
Apply the rule to legal cases
Work through the examples: (a) In the slave sale case, 'fa-huwa hurr' with 'fa' indicates acceptance and immediate freeing; without 'fa', it is not acceptance. (b) In the tailor case, 'fa-qta'uh' makes the cutting conditional on sufficiency; without 'fa', it is unconditional.
These cases demonstrate the practical impact of 'fa' in contract law.
5
Recognize the exceptional case where 'fa' enters on the cause
The passage notes that sometimes 'fa' enters on the cause, e.g., 'abshir fa-qad ataka al-ghawth'. This is contrary to the original principle, which expects 'fa' to enter on the effect.
This exception is important for interpreting texts where 'fa' introduces the reason rather than the result.

Source Text

ولد الا اولاد اپنے والد کا بدل احسان ادا نہیں کر سکتی ہاں اسے مملوک پائے اور خرید کرے پس ازاد کرے تو اس کے ساتھ مکافات ہو سکتی ہے کیونکہ والد بسبب تربیت کے اپنی اولاد کی حیوۃ حقیقیہ کا سبب ہے اور اپنے والد کو ازاد کرنے کے ساتھ اس کی حیوۃ حکمیہ کا سبب ہو جائے گا کیونکہ رق حکمی دورت ہے اب بیشتر یہ علہ ہے یعتقہ کی دونوں مفہوماً متغایر ہیں لیکن وجو دا یہی شرار عتق ہے خان قال بعت هذا العبد منك فقال الآخر فهو حر تو یہ قبول بیع ہوگا اور غلام آزاد ہو جائے گا کیونکہ اعتاق مستر ایجاب بیع پر مرتب نہیں ہوتا بلکہ بعد ثبوت القبول ہوتا ہے لہذا اس نے یوں کہا قبلت تصویر بخلاف هو حد اگر موخر کہتا ہے اور اس پر فاداخل نہیں کرتا تو یہ قبول بیچ نہیں ہو گا یہ ویسے خبر ہوگی اس امر کی کہ وہ تو حر ہے لہذا اس کی بیع کیسے ہو سکتی ہے اور فاتعقیبیہ کی وجہ سے نمو حر ما قبل پر متفرع ہو رہا تھا اور اس کی تفریح بغیر قبولیت بیع کے نہیں ہو سکتی ولو قال للخياط اگر خیاط سے کہتا ہے ایکفینی هذا الثوب قميصا کیا یہ کپڑا میری قمیص کے لئے کافی ہوگا خیاط کہتا ہے نعم تو صاحب الثوب کہتا ہے ناقطعہ نقطعہ فاذا هو لا یفی کاٹنے کے بعد وہ کپڑا قمیص کے لئے کافی نہیں ہوتا تو خیاط پر ثوب کی من واجب ہوگئی کیونکہ فاقطعہ کی فاجزا ئیہ ہے جس سے مقصد یہ ہے ان کان کا فیا للقمیص فاقطعہ تو قطع کا امر معلق بشرط الکفایہ تھا تو عدم کفایہ کی صورت میں شرط قطع محقق نہیں ہے لہذا ضمان واجب ہوگی بخلاف قولہ اقطعہ اگر بغیر نا کے اقطعہ کہتا ہے تو عدم کفایتہ کی صورت میں ضمان نہیں آئے گی کیونکہ یہ امر مطلق ہے اور شرط کفایہ پر متفرع نہیں ہے ۔ وقد تدخل على العلل کبھی فاعلل پر بھی داخل ہوتی ہے جیسے ابشر فقد ا تا ک الغوث غوث امداد کا پہونچنا یہ علہ ہے البشر کے لیئے ۔ یہ فاراخل على العلة ونظیر اور اس کی نظیر ہے ادائی الفافانت حر اس کا معنی ہوگا لانك حر يعتق في المحال یہ فی الحال آزاد ہو جائے گا اس کا عشق معلق بالا دار نہیں ہوگا اور اسی طرح انزل فانت آمن میں بھی لفظ فا داخل علی العلم ہے۔ اعلم ان اصل الفاء فا چونکہ تعقیب کے لئے ہے اس لئے اندر اصل یہ ہے کہ داخل علی المعلول ہو کیونکہ معلول علیہ کے عقب میں آتا ہے۔ وانما تدخل على العلت فا کا علہ پر داخل ہونا خلاف اصل
Al-fa' is for immediate succession (ta'qib), meaning that the thing entered by fa occurs after what precedes it without delay. Therefore it enters the result (jaza) because the occurrence of the result follows the verification of the condition. So if he said 'in dakhalti hadhihi al-dar fa-hadhihi al-dar fa-anti taliq' – the condition for divorce is that she enters these two houses sequentially, and if she enters the second without delay, then she becomes divorced. It enters the effect (ma'lul) because the effect occurs after the cause without delay. So it enters on the effect, like 'ja'a al-shita' fa-tajjab' – the coming of winter is the cause and preparation is the effect. Sometimes the effect is identical to the cause in existence but different in concept, like 'saqa fa-arwa' – 'saqa' means to water and 'arwa' means to quench, and in existence they are united, there is no difference. And like 'lan yajzi waladun' – a child cannot repay the kindness of his parents, but if he finds his parent enslaved and buys and frees him, then he can compensate, because the parent through upbringing is the cause of the child's real life, and by freeing his parent he becomes the cause of his legal life because legal slavery is removed. This is the cause of his freeing; both are different in concept but in existence they are the act of freeing. If he said 'ba'tu hadha al-'abda minka fa-qala al-akhar fa-huwa hurr' – this is acceptance of the sale and the slave becomes free because the freeing (i'taq) is not directly attached to the offer but occurs after the acceptance. So if he said 'qabiltu' it is different; if he said 'huwa hurr' without 'fa', it is not acceptance of sale but merely a statement that he is free, so how can sale occur? And because of 'fa ta'qibiyyah', it is subordinated to the preceding, which cannot happen without acceptance of sale. And if he said to the tailor 'akfini hadha al-thawba qamisan' (is this cloth sufficient for a shirt?), the tailor says 'na'am', then the owner says 'naqta'uh' (we cut it), and if after cutting the cloth is insufficient, then the tailor is liable because 'fa' in 'fa-qta'uh' is for jaza, meaning the command to cut is conditional on sufficiency. So if sufficiency is absent, the condition for cutting is not fulfilled, so liability applies. Unlike if he said 'iqta'uh' without 'fa', then there is no liability because it is an absolute command not dependent on sufficiency. And sometimes fa enters on the cause, like 'abshir fa-qad ataka al-ghawth' – the arrival of help is the cause of good news. This is fa entering on the cause. And similar is 'ada'ika fa-anta hurr' – this means 'because you are free', and he becomes free immediately; it is not suspended on entering the house. And similarly 'anzil fa-anta amin' – here also fa enters on the cause. Know that the original of fa is for ta'qib, so the principle is that it enters on the effect, because the effect follows the cause. But entering on the cause is contrary to the principle.
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This passage is a comprehensive discussion of the particle 'fa' in Arabic grammar and usul al-fiqh, focusing on its primary meaning of immediate succession (ta'qib). The author explains that 'fa' typically enters on the effect (ma'lul) or the result (jaza) of a condition, because the effect follows the cause. Examples include conditional sentences (if you enter the house, then you are divorced) and causal sequences (winter comes, then preparation). The author also gives examples where the cause and effect are united in existence but differ in concept, like watering and quenching. In legal applications, the author analyzes cases from sales and freeing slaves: if someone says 'I sold this slave to you' and the other responds with 'fa-huwa hurr' (then he is free) using 'fa', it constitutes acceptance of the sale and the slave is freed, because the freeing follows the acceptance. But without 'fa', it is just a statement. Another example involves a tailor: if the owner says 'cut this cloth for a shirt' with a conditional 'fa', the tailor is liable if it doesn't fit; without 'fa', no liability. Finally, the author notes that 'fa' can sometimes enter on the cause (like 'abshir fa-qad ataka al-ghawth'), but this is contrary to the original principle. This nuanced understanding of 'fa' is critical for interpreting legal texts and contracts in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
تعقیب (ta'qib): Immediate succession; the thing after fa occurs without delay after the previous.
معلول (ma'lul): The effect that results from a cause.
علت ('illa): The cause that brings about the effect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example 'ba'tu hadha al-'abda minka fa-qala al-akhar fa-huwa hurr', why does the slave become free?
A) Because the seller's offer immediately frees the slave.
B) Because the buyer's response with 'fa' indicates acceptance and the freeing follows immediately.
C) Because 'huwa hurr' is a declaration of freedom.
D) Because the waw is not used.

Key Takeaway
The particle 'fa' primarily indicates immediate succession (ta'qib) and enters on the effect, but it can exceptionally enter on the cause—a nuance vital for interpreting legal texts in Hanafi usul.

The Hanafi Rule of 'Fa' for Immediate Sequence and Its Exception
Explaining the Hanafi usul principle of 'fa' for immediate sequence and its teleological exception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify cases where 'fa' indicates immediate sequence (ta'qib) on the effect, and apply the Hanafi exception where 'fa' enters upon the cause due to a final cause (illa gha'iyya).

Core Idea
The 'fa' of immediate sequence normally links cause and effect; it can enter upon the cause when the cause is actually an effect of a higher purpose.

In Hanafi usul, the particle 'fa' (ف) is primarily used to indicate that an effect follows its cause without delay. This principle is crucial for deriving legal rulings from conditional and causal statements. However, there is a subtle exception: 'fa' can sometimes enter upon the cause itself when the cause is viewed as a means to a higher end (final cause). Understanding this exception prevents misinterpretation of texts in the Quran, hadith, and legal examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two clauses
Read the sentence and separate the two clauses connected by 'fa'. Determine which clause expresses a cause ('illa) and which expresses an effect (ma'lul).
The cause is that which brings about the other; the effect is what results from the cause.
2
Check where 'fa' enters
Normally, 'fa' enters upon the effect (ma'lul). If it appears to enter upon the cause, note that this is an exception to the general rule.
The default is that 'fa' attaches to the effect because the effect follows the cause.
3
Analyze the teleological structure
If 'fa' enters upon the cause, examine whether the cause is actually a means to an end (final cause). The primary intent (مقصود بالذات) of the speaker may shift the cause-effect relationship.
For example, in 'rejoice, for help has come', the primary intent is rejoicing, so rejoicing becomes the final cause and 'help has come' becomes the effect teleologically.
4
Apply the usul rule
Derive the legal ruling based on the correct identification: if 'fa' indicates immediate sequence on the effect, the ruling is immediate. If it enters upon the cause due to a final cause, the ruling still holds as a sequential particle because the cause is now an effect in a higher chain.
The Hanafi school relies on this linguistic precision to determine issues like liability, validity of contracts, and conditional obligations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
داخل ہو تو تب مطلقہ ہوگی تد تدخل على المعلول چونکہ معلول علقہ کے عقب بلا تراخی واقع ہوتا ہے اس نئے داخل على المعلول ہوتی ہے جیسے جاء الشتاء فتا جب تو موسم شتاء کا آنا یہ سبب وعلہ ہے تیار رہنے کا۔ تد يكون المعلول عين العلة في الوجود کبھی علم اور معلول باعتبار وجود کے متحد ہوتے ہیں لیکن مفہوما متغایر ہوتے ہیں جیسے سقاء فارواہ تو سقی علیہ اروار ہے مفہوما متغایر ہیں سقی کے معنی سے پلانا اور ارواہ کے بیع کیسے ہو سکتی ہے اور فاتعقیبیہ کی وجہ سے نمو حر ما قبل پر متفرع ہو رہا تھا اور اس کی تفریح بغیر قبولیت بیع کے نہیں ہو سکتی ولو قال للخياط اگر خیاط سے کہتا ہے ایکفینی هذا الثوب قميصا کیا یہ کپڑا عدم کفایتہ کی صورت میں ضمان نہیں آئے گی کیونکہ یہ امر مطلق ہے اور شرط کفایہ پر متفرع نہیں ہے ۔ وقد تدخل على العلل کبھی فاعلل پر بھی داخل ہوتی ہے جیسے ابشر فقد ا تاک الغوث غوث امداد کا پہونچنا یہ علہ پر داخل ہونا خلاف اصل تھا اس لئے اس کی توجیہ کر رہا ہے کہ علیہ پر اس وقت داخل ہوگی جب علہ سے مقصد معاون ہو تو علہ کے لئے یہ معلول علتہ غائیہ ہو جائے گا فیصير العلة معلولا تو اس وقت میں علم معلول کے درجہ میں ہو جائے گی اور معلول جو علہ غانیہ تھا یہ درجہ علہ میں ہو جائے گا اصل یہ ہے کہ علیہ عائیہ باعتبار وجود ذہنی کے فاعلیتہ فاعل کے لئے علہ ہوتی ہے لیکن باعتبار وجود خارجی کے معلول ہوتی ہے فقدا تاک الغوث میں نا داخل علی العلہ ہے لیکن چونکہ اس سے مقصود اصلی ابشار ہے لہذا وہ علہ غائیہ کے درجہ میں ہو کر اتاک الغوث کے لئے علت ہوگا اور اتاک الغوت معلول کے درجہ میں ہوگا اس لئے اس پر فاداخل ہوتی ہے ۔ فلهذا تدخل على العلة یعنی اس وقت فا کا دخول علی العلة باعتبار اس کے نہ ہوا کہ وہ علہ ہے بلکہ باعتبار اس کے معلول ہونے کے ہوا تو دخول فا تعقیبہ صحیح ہوگا ومن ذلك قوله تعالى وتزر دوا نان خير الزاد التقوى یعنی جب سفر حج کرو تو اپنے ساتھ زاد راہ لے جایا کرو کیونکہ اس کی بھلائی تقوی ہے یعنی سوال اور گداگری سے بچ جاؤ گے تو خیر الزاد التقوی کہنے سے مقصود بالذات یہ ہے کہ یہ لوگ اپنے ساتھ زاد راہ لے جائیں یہ نہ ہو کہ متوکل علی اللہ بن کر نکالیں پھر حرم میں پہونچ کر لوگوں سے سوال کرتے پھریں تو جب تزودوا مقصود بالذات ہوا تو یہ خیر الزاد کی علت غنائیہ ہو جائے گا اور خیر الزاد بدرجہ معلول اس لئے اس پر فاداخل ہوتی ہے وقول الشاعر اذا ملك لم یکن ذاهبة یعنی جب بادشاہ صاحب حبہ نہ ہو یعنی لوگوں پر ہیہ اور عطیہ نہ کرے تو فدعه فدولته ذاهبة پس اسے چھوڑ دے کہ اس کی دولت وسلطنت جانیوالی اور ضائع ہونیوالی ہے یعنی اس شعر سے مقصود بالذات یہی بتلانا ہے کہ جب وہ شخص صاحب ہیہ نہ ہو تو یہ علہ غانیہ کے درجہ میں ہو جائے گی۔ اور دولتہ ذاهبہ بدرجہ معلول تو فا اس لئے اس پر داخل ہوئی ہے ۔ یا اس کی توجیہ یوں بھی ہو سکتی ہے کہ فاعلل پر اس وقت داخل ہوئی جب علل کو دوام حاصل ہو تو اس وقت یہ ابتدائے حکم سے متراخی ہو جاتی ہے۔ اس لئے نام تعقیبی ان پر داخل ہو جاتی ہے دانما قلنا یعتق فی الحال ہم نے جو کہا ہے کہ اوالی الفا فانت حرم میں غلام فی الحال ازاد ہو جائے گا اس کی وجہ یہ ہے کہ فائنت حرہ کا معنی ہے لانک حرو، تو اس صورت میں فانت حر امر کا یعنی از کا جواب نہیں بن سکتا ۔ لان جواب الامر کیونکہ امر کا جواب صرف فعل مضارع ہوتا ہے وجہ اس کی یہ پیش کی کہ امر مستحق جواب بتقدیران شرطیہ ہوتا ہے اور کلمہ ان ماضی کو بمعنی مستقبل کر دیتا ہے والجملة الاسمية اور جملہ اسمیہ جودالہ علی الثبوت ہوتا ہے وہ بھی بمعنی مستقبل ہوتا ہے۔ وانما تجعل ذلك اور یہ اس وقت ہوتا ہے جب لفظ ان ملفوظ ہو۔ اما اذا كانت مقدر ہے اور جب کلمتہ ان مقدر ہو تو پھر ماضی کو بمعنی مستقبل نہیں کر سکتا ۔ كما تقول ان تأتني اكرمتك تو اس مثال میں لفظ ان ملفوظ ہے تو ماضی جو اس کے جواب ہے
If it enters, then it will be absolute. 'Fa' enters upon the effect because the effect occurs after the cause without delay. Thus it enters upon the effect, like 'winter came, so we prepared' — the coming of winter is the cause for preparing. Sometimes the effect is identical to the cause in existence but different in meaning, like 'saqa' and 'arwa' — both refer to giving drink but 'saqa' means to give water while 'arwa' means to satiate, yet in existence they are the same. Similarly, 'a child cannot recompense its parent' — if a child finds a parent enslaved and buys and frees him, then he can recompense because the parent is the cause of the child's real life through upbringing, and freeing the parent becomes the cause of the parent's legal life since legal servitude is removed. So the cause 'freeing him' and the effect 'freedom' are different in meaning but identical in existence. Also, 'if someone says: I sold this slave to you, and the other says: then he is free' — this is acceptance of sale and the slave becomes free because emancipation is not built upon the offer of sale but after the acceptance is established. Hence he said 'I accepted' contrary to if the second says 'he is free' without 'fa', it is not acceptance of sale but a mere statement that he is free, so the sale cannot occur. And if one says to a tailor: 'Is this cloth sufficient for my shirt?' The tailor says 'yes', the cloth owner says 'then cut it', but after cutting it is not sufficient, then the tailor is liable because the command to cut is conditional on sufficiency. But if he says 'cut it' without 'fa', there is no liability because the command is absolute. And sometimes 'fa' enters upon the cause, like 'rejoice, for help has come to you' — the arrival of help is the cause for rejoicing. This is 'fa' entering upon the cause. Similarly, 'take this [slave], for you are free' means 'because you are free, he is free immediately,' not delayed. And likewise in 'descend, for you are safe' — 'fa' enters upon the cause. Know that the original of 'fa' is for sequence, so it should enter upon the effect because the effect follows the cause. Its entering upon the cause is contrary to the original. Hence the explanation is that 'fa' enters upon the cause when the cause becomes a means to an end — then the cause becomes an effect in terms of purpose, so the cause becomes like an effect and the effect like a cause. Thus 'fa' correctly indicates sequence. This is why in 'rejoice, for help has come', though 'fa' enters upon 'help has come' which is the cause, the primary intent is rejoicing, so rejoicing becomes the final cause and 'help has come' becomes the effect, so 'fa' applies. Similarly in the verse 'and take provisions, for the best provision is piety' — the primary intent is to take provisions, so 'take provisions' is the final cause and 'the best provision is piety' becomes the effect, hence 'fa' enters. And in the poet's line 'when a king is not generous, then leave him, for his state will perish' — the primary intent is to indicate that if he is not generous, that is the final cause, and 'his state will perish' is the effect. Alternatively, it can be explained that 'fa' enters upon the cause when the cause has perpetuity, then it becomes remote from the initial command, so the sequential 'fa' enters. We have said that in the phrase 'take this, for you are free', the slave is free immediately because the meaning is 'because you are free', so 'for you are free' cannot be the answer to the command because the answer to the command must be a verb, since the command implies a conditional 'if', and 'in' makes the past tense future, but a nominal sentence also denotes continuity and can be future. This only works when 'in' is explicit, but if it is implied, then the past tense cannot be future, as in 'if you come, I honor you' where 'in' is explicit and the past tense is its answer.
Source pages p. 342

Commentary
This passage is an expanded commentary on the usul principle governing the use of the letter 'fa' (ف) in Arabic conditional and causal constructions. The author, following the Hanafi method, explains that the primary function of 'fa' is to indicate immediate sequence (ta'qib) of the effect (ma'lul) upon the cause ('illa). However, there is an exception: 'fa' can also enter upon the cause when the cause itself is viewed as a means to an end, such that it becomes like an effect in relation to a higher purpose. The commentator provides multiple examples from fiqh, Quran, and poetry to illustrate this. Key points: (1) In 'I sold you this slave' and the reply 'then he is free' with 'fa', the 'fa' indicates that the freedom is consequent upon the acceptance of sale, so it is a valid acceptance. (2) In the tailor example, 'then cut it' (faqta'hu) creates a conditional liability, while a plain command 'cut it' (iqta') does not. (3) In 'rejoice, for help has come', the 'fa' enters upon the cause ('help has come') but because the primary intent is rejoicing, the cause becomes an effect in the teleological sense, allowing 'fa' to work correctly. (4) The Quranic verse 'take provisions, for the best provision is piety' is analyzed similarly: the command to take provisions is the real purpose, so it becomes the final cause; 'the best provision is piety' becomes the effect. (5) The poet's line about a king who is not generous: the clause 'then leave him' is the main point, and 'his state will perish' is the effect. The commentator also clarifies that when 'fa' is followed by a nominal sentence (as in 'fa anta hurr'), it does not serve as the answer to a conditional command because the answer must be a verb, unless the conditional particle 'in' is explicit and transforms the past tense into future meaning. This is a subtle point of Arabic grammar and usul. The entire discussion reinforces the Hanafi reliance on linguistic precision in deriving legal rulings.

Key Terms
فاء التعقيب (fa' al-ta'qib): The 'fa' that indicates immediate sequence of the effect after the cause without delay.
العلة والمعلول (al-illa wa al-ma'lul): The cause and its effect; in usul, the cause ('illa) is the reason or condition that brings about the ruling (ma'lul).
علة غائية (illa gha'iyya): A final cause: the end or purpose for which an action is done, considered as a cause in the mental realm.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi analysis, why does 'fa' enter upon the cause in the phrase 'ابشر فقد اتاك الغوث' (Rejoice, for help has come)?
A) Because the grammar of Arabic requires 'fa' to be followed by a nominal clause.
B) Because the primary intent is rejoicing, making it the final cause and 'help has come' the effect.
C) Because 'fa' can enter upon any clause that is causal in nature.
D) Because 'rejoice' is a command, and 'fa' always follows commands.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi usul principle of 'fa' is that it primarily indicates immediate sequence on the effect, but can enter upon the cause when the cause is a means to a higher end (final cause), preserving the sequential meaning.

Explicit vs Implicit 'in': Tense Conversion in Conditional Clauses
Linguistic analysis of 'in' and its effect on tense

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish explicit and implicit 'in' and apply the rule that implicit 'in' does not convert past to future, and identify correct answers to commands.

Core Idea
Explicit 'in' makes past future; implicit 'in' does not — nominal sentences are even less convertible.

This lesson analyzes a key linguistic distinction in Hanafi usul: the behavior of the conditional particle 'in' when explicit versus implicit. The passage uses examples from commands and nominal sentences to show that an implicit 'in' cannot convert a past tense verb to future meaning, and by analogy, cannot convert a nominal sentence either. This rule impacts how we understand the answer to a command in legal texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the particle 'in' and its form
Distinguish between explicit 'in' (pronounced) and implicit 'in' (understood). The passage contrasts these in examples like 'in tatini' versus the implied 'in' in the answer to a command.
Implicit 'in' is grammatically assumed but not uttered.
2
Examine the rule: explicit vs implicit effect
Explicit 'in' converts a past tense verb to future meaning. Implicit 'in' does not. The passage states that the answer to a command carries an implicit 'in', so the verb must be present, not past.
Example: 'taeni akramtuka' is wrong because past tense 'akramtuka' is not converted.
3
Apply to commands
In the answer to an imperative, an implicit 'in' is assumed. Therefore, the verb must be in present/future tense. The correct form is 'taeni akramuka'.
The passage emphasizes 'wajib' (obligatory) to use present tense.
4
Extend to nominal sentences by qiyas
The passage uses qiyas jali: if implicit 'in' cannot convert a past verb (which is close to future as a verb), then it even less can convert a nominal sentence (which denotes a state far from future). Thus nominal sentences cannot serve as the answer to a command with implicit 'in'.
This is a 'bi-tariq awa' (primary analogy) argument.
5
Conclude with practical fiqh example
The passage ends with a manumission case: if a master says 'taeni fa-anta hurrun' (come to me, so you are free), the slave is freed immediately because the nominal sentence cannot be conditional on the command. The alif (payment) is not required.
This shows the direct legal consequence of the linguistic rule.

Source Text
