Iqtida' al-Nas: Definition and the Sale Example
Definition and application of iqtida' al-nas with sale example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to DEFINE iqtida' al-nas and APPLY it to derive an implied sale from the command 'Free your slave on my behalf for one thousand'.

Core Idea
When a text necessarily requires a condition for validity, that condition is implied even if unstated.

In this lesson, we explore the Hanafi usul concept of iqtida' al-nas. The passage introduces this principle through a classic example: a person commands another to free his slave for a payment. The command to free implies a sale, because ownership must be transferred to the commander. The text then explains what conditions of that implied sale are necessary. This fundamental tool allows us to derive rulings that are logically required for the validity of an explicit command.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Text and the Command
Take the statement 'Free your slave on my behalf for one thousand.' Recognize that it is a command (amr) to perform an act (manumission) with a condition (for one thousand).
2
Recognize the Necessity
Consider what is necessary for the validity of the command: the commander cannot free a slave he does not own. Ownership must be transferred to him. Therefore, a sale is necessarily implied.
3
Apply Iqtida' al-Nas
The text requires the existence of a sale even though it is not explicitly mentioned. This is the operation of iqtida' al-nas.
4
Limit the Implied Conditions
Not all conditions of a normal sale are required; only those that cannot be dropped (like mutual consent) are necessary. Conditions that can be dropped (like verbal acceptance in ta'ati) are not required here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وھذا المقتضی کلام مقتضی کی مثال دى بقوله نحو اعتق عبدك عنى بالف يقتضى البيع یہ کلام تقاضا کرتی ہے بیع کا تو اعتق عبدک عنی بالف یہ مقتضی ہے اور بیع اس کا مقتضا ہے۔ ضرورة صحة العتق کیونکہ آمر کی جانب سے صحتہ عتق اس کے بغیر ہو نہیں سکتی کیونکہ ایک شخص اپنے غلام کو دوسرے شخص کی جانب سے وکالتاً و نیابتاً آزاد نہیں کر سکتا جب تک اس دوسرے شخص کے ملک میں نہ دیدے اور وہ ملک اس مقام پر بیع ہی ہو سکتی ہے اور بالف یہ قرینہ بیع ہے کہ مبادلۃ المال بالمال ہے لہذا بیع اس کا مقتضی ہے اور عتق سے یہ مقدم ہو گی تو ایسی شی جو لازمی ہو اور ہو مقدم اس پر دلالت کلام کو اقتضاء النص کہا جاتا ہے ۔ نص کا کہنا گویا کہ اس کلام کا مفہوم یہ ہوا کہ أعتق عبدك عني بألف وكن وكيلي في الاعتاق تو عنی یہ حال من الفاعل ہے اور بالف کی جار مجرور اعتق کے ساتھ متعلق ہے اس میں معنی بیع کی تضمین ہوگی کأنه قال أعتقه عني مبيعا مني بألف فيثبت ههنا البيع بقدر الضرورة اور یہاں ثبوت بیع صرف بقدر ضرورت ہو گا اور یہ بیع اقتضائی اس بیع کے برابر نہیں ہے جو صراحۃ لفظ بالبیع سے ثابت ہوتی ہے۔ لہذا اس کے لئے جمیع شرائط کا ثبوت ضروری نہیں بلکہ اس کے ارکان اور وہ شروط ثابت ہوں گے جو احتمال سقوط نہیں رکھتے اور جو فی الجملہ ساقط ہونے کا احتمال رکھتے ہیں یہاں ان کا ثبوت ضروری نہیں ہے جیسے مشتری کی جانب سے قبول باوجود رکن بیع ہونے کے یہ یہاں ضروری نہیں کیونکہ بیع بالتعاطی میں یہ ساقط ہو سکتا ہے اور اسی طرح خیار رؤیت اور خیار عیب وغیرہ بھی ثابت نہیں ہوں گے کیونکہ یہ محتمل سقوط ہیں ۔ فقال أبو یوسف یہ جو کہا کہ اقتضاء النص ملفوظ صریح جیسا نہیں ہوتا اور اس میں جمیع شروط کا ثبوت ضروری نہیں یہ اسی پر تفریع ہے اور یہ تفریع صرف امام ابو یوسف کے نزدیک ہے اور طرفین کے نزدیک نہیں ہے. لو قال اعتق عبدك عني بغير شيء یعنی اگر ایک شخص دوسرے کو کہتا ہے کہ اپنا غلام میری جانب سے بغیر کسی عوض کے مفت آزاد کر دے تو ابو یوسف کے نزدیک یہ عتق آمر کی جانب سے صحیح ہو جائیگا لیکن طرفین کے نزدیک یہ عتق آمر کی جانب سے صحیح نہیں بلکہ از جانب مامور عتق واقع ہو جائیگا امام ابو یوسف یہاں اقتضاء النص کے طور پر پہلے سیہ ثابت کریں گے پھر عتق کو صحیح قرار دیں گے چونکہ ہبہ بلا قبض صحیح نہیں ہوتا تو انھوں نے اس کو صورت سابقہ بیع والی پر قیاس کیا کہ جیسے قبول من المشتری ساقط ہو چکا ہے تو یہاں بھی تستغنى الهبة عن القبض وهو شرط فاستغنى عن القبض ہو جائیگا اور یہ شرط ساقط ہو جائیگی جیسے بیع کی صورت میں قبول ساقط ہو گیا ہے حالانکہ وہ رکن بیع تھا اور ہبہ میں قبض رکن نہیں ہے بلکہ شرط ہے لہذا یہ ساقط ہو جائیگی۔ ولنا يسقط ما يحتمل السقوط به امام ابو یوسف کے قیاس علی البیع کا جواب ہے خلاصہ یہ کہ وہ اشیاء ساقط ہوتی ہیں جو احتمال سقوط رکھیں والقبول هما يحتمله قبول البیع لساناً از جانب مشتری محتمل سقوط ہے کما فی التعاطی جیسے بیع بالتعاطی میں کہ قیمت طے شدہ بیع کے ہاتھ میں دیگر مبیع اٹھا کر مشتری چلا جائے تو بیع صحیح ہے و لسانا قبول کرنے کی ضرورت نہیں ہے لا القبض اور قبض فی الهبة کبھی ساقط نہیں ہو سکتا اور یہ محتمل سقوط فی الطبہ نہیں ہے لہذا قیاس مع الفارق ہے لہذا اس صورت میں عتق عن الامر صحیح نہیں ہوگا کیونکہ یہ ملک آمر نہیں ہے اور اس صورت میں عتق
And this is iqtida'. An example of iqtida' is his saying: 'Free your slave on my behalf for one thousand,' which requires a sale. This speech demands a sale, so 'Free your slave on my behalf for one thousand' is the iqtida' (requiring thing), and sale is its muqtada (required thing), due to the necessity of the validity of the manumission, because the validity of the manumission from the command's side cannot happen without it, because a person cannot free his slave on behalf of another by agency or representation until he gives him ownership, and that ownership can only be through sale here, and 'for one thousand' is an indication of sale because it is exchange of money for money. Therefore, sale is the muqtada, and it takes priority over manumission. So something that is necessary and takes priority is called iqtida' al-nas (implication of the text). The text says, as if the meaning of this speech is: 'I free your slave on my behalf for one thousand, and be my agent in the freeing.' So 'on my behalf' is a circumstantial accusative, and 'for one thousand' is related to 'free'; in it there is an implication of the meaning of sale, as if he said: 'Free him on my behalf as sold to me for one thousand.' So sale is established here to the extent of necessity, and this sale due to iqtida' is not equal to a sale explicitly established by the word 'sale'. Therefore, it is not necessary that all conditions be established; rather, its pillars and those conditions that do not admit annulment are established, while those that possibly can be annulled are not necessary to establish here, such as acceptance from the buyer, which, despite being a pillar of sale, is not necessary here because it can be dropped in sale by mutual exchange (ta'ati). Similarly, option of inspection and option of defect etc. are also not established because they are subject to annulment. Then Abu Yusuf said: This statement that iqtida' al-nas is not like an explicit wording and that all conditions are not necessary to prove—this is a derivation upon that, and this derivation is only according to Imam Abu Yusuf, not according to the two (Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad). If someone says: 'Free your slave on my behalf for nothing,' meaning a person tells another to free his slave on his behalf without any compensation, freely, then according to Abu Yusuf, this manumission is valid on behalf of the commander, but according to the two, it is not valid on behalf of the commander; rather, the manumission occurs from the commanded person. Imam Abu Yusuf, on the basis of iqtida' al-nas, will first establish the [ownership?] and then validate the manumission. Since hibah (gift) without possession is not valid, he analogized it to the previous sale case: just as acceptance from the buyer was dropped, here too the gift can dispense with possession, and this condition is dropped, just as acceptance was dropped in sale even though it was a pillar, while possession in gift is not a pillar but a condition, so it is dropped. Our response (to Abu Yusuf's analogy on sale) is that only things that admit annulment are dropped. Acceptance of sale verbally from the buyer is subject to annulment, as in exchange by ta'ati, where the buyer takes the goods after price is fixed and leaves, the sale is valid without verbal acceptance. But possession in gift can never be dropped, and it is not subject to annulment by nature. Therefore, the analogy is invalid. Hence, in this case, manumission on behalf of the commander is not valid because it is not the property of the commander, and in this case, manumission...
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage explains the usul concept of iqtida' al-nas (implication of the text) in Hanafi jurisprudence. The key example is when a person says to another: 'Free your slave on my behalf for one thousand dirhams.' The command to free implies the necessity of a sale, because the commander cannot free a slave he does not own; ownership must be transferred to him first, which requires a sale. This necessary condition (sale) is understood from the text even though not explicitly stated. This implication is called iqtida' al-nas. The passage specifies that such an implied sale is not equivalent to an explicit sale; it only has the minimal conditions necessary. For example, acceptance from the buyer is not required, as it can be dropped in sales by mutual exchange (ta'ati). However, there is an ikhtilaf: according to Imam Abu Yusuf, if someone says 'Free your slave on my behalf without any payment,' the manumission is still valid on behalf of the commander, by qiyas to the previous case—since possession (qabd) is a condition for gift (hibah) and can be dropped. But the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) disagree: they say that possession in gift is never subject to annulment, so the analogy fails. Thus, the manumission is not on behalf of the commander but from the commanded person. The passage presents Abu Yusuf's analogy and the rebuttal.

Key Terms
اقتضاء النص (iqtida' al-nas): The implication of the text that requires something necessary for the validity of the literal command, though not explicitly stated.
مقتضى (muqtada): That which is required by the iqtida' (e.g., a sale).
مقتضِ (muqtadi): The text that requires something (the implicate).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example 'Free your slave on my behalf for one thousand,' what does iqtida' al-nas require?
A) A gift
B) A sale
C) A loan
D) A pledge

Key Takeaway
Iqtida' al-nas allows us to infer conditions necessary for the validity of a command, but only to the extent of necessity, dropping dispensable requirements.

The Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Iqtida' in a Gift-Based Manumission
Intra-Hanafi dispute on iqtida' with gift-based manumission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to EXPLAIN the disagreement between Abu Yusuf and the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) regarding the application of iqtida' al-nas when the command is 'Free your slave on my behalf for nothing', and EVALUATE the rebuttal based on the principle that only conditions subject to annulment can be dropped.

Core Idea
In iqtida' al-nas, conditions that can be dropped (like acceptance in sale) are not required, but conditions that never drop (like possession in gift) must be fulfilled.

Building on the previous lesson's definition, this passage presents an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf. Abu Yusuf extends iqtida' to a case where the commander says 'Free your slave on my behalf for nothing' (a gift). He analogizes it to the sale case, claiming the condition of possession in gift can be dropped. The two (Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad) reject this, arguing that possession in gift is never dropable, thus the analogy fails. This lesson examines their disagreement and the underlying usul principle.

Guided Path
1
State the New Scenario
The commander says 'Free your slave on my behalf for nothing' – a gift-based manumission.
2
Present Abu Yusuf's Application
He uses iqtida' to imply a gift, then claims possession (qabd) is dropped by necessity, analogizing to dropping acceptance in sale.
3
Present the Rebuttal
The two respond: only conditions that admit annulment are dropped. Possession in gift does not admit annulment, so the analogy is invalid (qiyas ma'a al-fariq).
4
Identify the Thamarah al-Khilaf
Practical consequence: under Abu Yusuf, the manumission is valid on behalf of the commander; under the two, it is not – the slave is freed from the commanded person.
5
Determine the Mufta Bihi
The text indicates the two's position is correct, as the rebuttal is presented as 'our' response.

And this is iqtida'. An example of iqtida' is his saying: 'Free your slave on my behalf for one thousand,' which requires a sale. This speech demands a sale, so 'Free your slave on my behalf for one thousand' is the iqtida' (requiring thing), and sale is its muqtada (required thing), due to the necessity of the validity of the manumission, because the validity of the manumission from the command's side cannot happen without it, because a person cannot free his slave on behalf of another by agency or representation until he gives him ownership, and that ownership can only be through sale here, and 'for one thousand' is an indication of sale because it is exchange of money for money. Therefore, sale is the muqtada, and it takes priority over manumission. So something that is necessary and takes priority is called iqtida' al-nas (implication of the text). The text says, as if the meaning of this speech is: 'I free your slave on my behalf for one thousand, and be my agent in the freeing.' So 'on my behalf' is a circumstantial accusative, and 'for one thousand' is related to 'free'; in it there is an implication of the meaning of sale, as if he said: 'Free him on my behalf as sold to me for one thousand.' So sale is established here to the extent of necessity, and this sale due to iqtida' is not equal to a sale explicitly established by the word 'sale'. Therefore, it is not necessary that all conditions be established; rather, its pillars and those conditions that do not admit annulment are established, while those that possibly can be annulled are not necessary to establish here, such as acceptance from the buyer, which, despite being a pillar of sale, is not necessary here because it can be dropped in sale by mutual exchange (ta'ati). Similarly, option of inspection and option of defect etc. are also not established because they are subject to annulment. But possession in gift can never be dropped, and it is not subject to annulment by nature. Therefore, the analogy is invalid. Commentary
This passage explains the usul concept of iqtida' al-nas (implication of the text) in Hanafi jurisprudence. The key example is when a person says to another: 'Free your slave on my behalf for one thousand dirhams.' The command to free implies the necessity of a sale, because the commander cannot free a slave he does not own; ownership must be transferred to him first, which requires a sale. This necessary condition (sale) is understood from the text even though not explicitly stated. This implication is called iqtida' al-nas. The passage specifies that such an implied sale is not equivalent to an explicit sale; it only has the minimal conditions necessary. For example, acceptance from the buyer is not required, as it can be dropped in sales by mutual exchange (ta'ati). However, there is an ikhtilaf: according to Imam Abu Yusuf, if someone says 'Free your slave on my behalf without any payment,' the manumission is still valid on behalf of the commander, by qiyas to the previous case—since possession (qabd) is a condition for gift (hibah) and can be dropped. But the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) disagree: they say that possession in gift is never subject to annulment, so the analogy fails. Thus, the manumission is not on behalf of the commander but from the commanded person. The passage presents Abu Yusuf's analogy and the rebuttal.

Key Terms
اقتضاء النص (iqtida' al-nas): The implication of the text that requires something necessary for the validity of the literal command, though not explicitly stated.
هبة (hibah): Gift; a contract of gratuitous transfer of ownership.
قبض (qabd): Possession; taking physical control of an item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The scope of iqtida' al-nas is limited: only conditions that admit annulment (yahtamil al-suqut) can be dropped; otherwise, the analogy fails.

Suspended Divorce and Manumission on Future Ownership
Applies the usul principle of ta'liq to suspended divorce/manumission upon future ownership.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Shafi'i position on suspended divorce/manumission when ownership is absent at the time of utterance, and compare it with the Hanafi view, using the istinbat chain.

Core Idea
Shafi'i: conditioned clause creates immediate cause; missing ownership at utterance invalidates cause, so divorce never occurs.

This passage applies the earlier usul dispute about suspended rulings to a concrete branch: divorce or manumission suspended on future ownership. The author explains the Shafi'i derivation, showing how their principle that the conditioned (mashrut) is a complete speech leads to a specific ruling. Understanding this example clarifies how differences in usul principles produce different fiqh outcomes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the usul principle
Recognize the Shafi'i principle: the conditioned clause (mashrut) in a conditional sentence is a complete speech that creates an immediate cause (sabab) for the ruling.
This contrasts with the Hanafi view that the conditional sentence is a single unit, so no cause exists until the condition is fulfilled.
2
Apply the principle to the case
Take the utterance 'If I marry you, you are divorced' (قال: إن مکتک أو نکحتک فأنت طالق). According to Shafi'i, the phrase 'you are divorced' stands as a complete cause for divorce at the moment of utterance.
The suspension (ta'liq) only delays the ruling, not the cause.
3
Note the condition of ownership
Ownership (milk) at the time of the cause is a condition for the cause's validity by consensus. Since at utterance the woman is ajnabiyya (not owned), ownership is absent.
This condition is agreed upon by both Hanafi and Shafi'i.
4
Determine the outcome in Shafi'i
Because ownership is missing, the cause is invalid. Thus, even if the man later marries the woman, divorce does not occur. The ta'liq is void.
The ruling is that no divorce takes place.
5
Contrast with the Hanafi view
In Hanafi usul, the cause does not exist at utterance; it arises only when the condition is fulfilled. Since ownership exists at that future moment, the cause is valid and divorce occurs upon marriage.
This difference stems from the classification of the conditional statement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هے امام شافعی کے نز دیک انعقد سببها وقوع طلاق کا سبب منعقد ہو چکا ہے۔ لکن التعلیق لیکن تعلیق نے وقوع طلاق کے حکم کو موخرالی زمان وجود الشرط کر دیا ہے اسی وعلی ما ذكرنا امام شافعی کے مذہب کی توضیح ہے کہ ان کے نزدیک جیسے ہم نے ذکر کیا ہے کہ مشروط بدون الشرط جميع تقادیر پر موجب حکم ہے اور تعلیق نے اس حکم کو تقدیر معین کے ساتھ مقید کر دیا ہے واعدم الحکم اور اسی تعلیق نے اس حکم کو تقدیر معین کے علاوہ باقی تقدیر پر محروم کر دیا ہے گویا کہ یہ عدم ان کے نزدیک مفہوم من لنص ہوا تب تو وہ اس کو حکم نہیں قرانہ دے کہ تقدیر کے قائل ہوئے تو ان دخلت الدار فانت طالق حکم نہ میں فصار انت طالق انت طالق سبب محکم ہو چکا ہے ويكون تاثیر التعلیق اور تعلیق کی تاثیر صرف تاخیر حکم میں ہے لانی منع السببية تعليق سببیہ کو نہیں روکتی سبب للحکم موجود ہے اور حکم صرف موثر ہے الى وجود الشرط - فالطل تعليق الطلاق والعتاق بالملك هذا تفریح یہ اس پر تفریح ہے کہ مشروہ جو معلق بالشرط ہے فانعقد سبب یہ سبب للحكم منعقد اور موجود ہو چکا ہے عنده ای عند الشافعی نان وجود الملك اور وجود ملک شرط ہے بوقت وجود سبب کے بالاتفاق اگر وجود سبب کے وقت ملک نہ ہوا تو سبب باطل ہو جائیگا۔ و المعلق العقد سبب عمدہ امام شافعی کے نزدیک معلق یعنی مشروط سبب ـ للحكم بن چکا ہے اور موجود ہو چکا ہے ناذا علق الطلاق والعتاق جب طلاق یا عتاق کو معلق بالملک کرتا ہے مثلا ایک اجنبیہ عورت کو کہتا ہے ان مکتک یا نکحتک فانت طالق اور ایک غلام جو اس کا مملوک نہیں ہے اسے کہتا ہے ان مملکتک فانت حر تو یہ معلق ان کے نزدیک سبب منعقد ہو کر موجود ہو چکا ہے لیکن اس وقت ملک موجود نہیں ہے لہذا ان کے نزدیک اس قسم کی تعلیقات باطل ہیں لہذا اس کے بعد وہ عورت اس کے نکاح میں اور وہ غلام اس کے ملک میں آجائے تو طلاق اور عتق واقع نہیں ہوگا۔
According to Imam al-Shafi'i, its cause has been concluded; the cause of divorce has been concluded. However, the suspension has delayed the ruling of divorce until the time of existence of the condition. This is the clarification of Imam al-Shafi'i's madhhab according to what we have mentioned: that in his view, as we said, the conditioned without the condition is obligator of the ruling upon all circumstances, and the suspension has restricted this ruling to a specific circumstance, and has deprived the ruling on remaining circumstances. So this negation for him is a meaning from the text. Then he considered it a ruling from the Quran; he believed in the circumstance. So (in) 'If you enter the house, you are divorced', the ruling (is established) and 'you are divorced' becomes a firm cause. And the effect of the suspension is only in delaying the ruling, because suspension does not prevent causality; the cause for the ruling exists, and the ruling is only delayed until the existence of the condition. So this is a branch: suspension of divorce and manumission upon ownership. This is a branch upon that: that the conditioned which is suspended upon a condition, its cause has been concluded and exists, according to him, i.e., according to al-Shafi'i, because existence of ownership is a condition at the time of existence of the cause by consensus. If at the time of existence of the cause ownership does not exist, then the cause becomes invalid. And the suspended contract as a cause—according to Imam al-Shafi'i, the suspended, i.e., the conditioned, has become a cause for the ruling and is existent. So when he suspends divorce or manumission upon ownership, for example, saying to a foreign woman 'If I marry you (or: if I possess you), you are divorced', or to a slave who is not his property, 'If I possess you, you are free', then this suspended, in his view, has concluded as a cause and is existent, but at that time ownership does not exist. Therefore, in his view, these kinds of suspensions are invalid; accordingly, if later that woman enters his marriage or that slave enters his ownership, divorce or manumission does not occur.
Source pages p. 422

Commentary
This passage applies the earlier usul dispute to a specific fiqh branch: the suspension of divorce or manumission upon future ownership (ta'liq al-talaq wa al-'itaq bi al-milk). The author explains Imam al-Shafi'i's position: in a suspended condition like 'If I marry you, you are divorced', the very utterance of the suspended clause (the mashrut) creates an immediate cause (sabab) for the divorce. The suspension (ta'liq) only delays the ruling (the actual divorce) until the condition (marriage) occurs. However, if at the time of the utterance the person does not own the woman or slave (i.e., she is ajnabiyya or not his slave), then according to al-Shafi'i, the cause is concluded but ownership is a prerequisite for the cause's validity; since ownership is absent, the cause is invalid, and thus no divorce or manumission occurs even if ownership is later acquired. The Hanafi position is implicitly the opposite: since the conditioned is not a complete speech without the condition, no cause is concluded until the condition is met; thus if the condition is impossible (like marrying a foreign woman), the suspension is void and no divorce can later occur. The passage emphasizes that the effect of suspension in al-Shafi'i's view is purely in delaying the ruling, not in preventing the cause.

Key Terms
سبب (sabab): The cause that generates a ruling; here, the utterance of the suspended clause is considered a cause for divorce.
تعليق (ta'liq): Suspending a ruling upon a condition; its effect is to delay the ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Shafi'i view presented in the passage, if a man says to a foreign woman 'If I marry you, you are divorced' and later marries her, does divorce occur?
A) Yes, because the condition was fulfilled.
B) No, because the cause was invalid due to lack of ownership at the time of utterance.
C) Yes, because the utterance created a valid cause.
D) No, because the suspension delays both cause and ruling.

Key Takeaway
In Shafi'i usul, the conditioned clause creates an immediate cause; missing ownership at utterance invalidates the cause, so no divorce occurs even if ownership later acquired.

Pre-Fulfillment of Suspended Vows (Nadhr Mu'allaq)
Applies the principle of cause and obligation to suspended vows.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the Shafi'i istinbat chain for pre-fulfillment of a suspended vow and identify the key usul principles of cause (sabab) and obligation (wujub).

Core Idea
A suspended vow creates a cause immediately, making pre-fulfillment valid by qiyas to zakat.

This lesson examines a specific application of the principle that performing an act after its cause but before its obligation is valid. The case of a suspended vow (nadhr mu'allaq) illustrates how the Shafi'i school derives permissibility to fulfill the vow before the condition occurs, using qiyas to zakat paid before the year. Understanding this reasoning sharpens your ability to trace usul chains and compare schools.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and example
The passage rules that it is permissible (ja'iz) to expedite fulfillment of a suspended vow, e.g., fasting three days before the sick person is cured.
The Arabic example: 'If Allah cures the sick, I owe fasting three days.'
2
Determine the cause (sabab) from the utterance
The mere utterance of the suspended vow creates a cause for the obligation, even though the obligation itself is delayed until the condition is met.
Cause = vow utterance; condition = healing.
3
Apply the principle of pre-fulfillment after cause
Performing the act after the cause exists but before the obligation is due is valid by consensus (e.g., paying zakat after owning nisab but before the year passes).
This principle is established earlier in the text.
4
Verify by analogy (qiyas) to zakat
Just as zakat after ownership but before the year is valid, so is the vow after utterance but before the condition.
The shared illa is that the cause has already occurred.
5
Recognize the cross-madhhab implication
The Hanafi school would reject this, arguing that no cause exists until the condition is fulfilled. This dispute roots in differing usul views on the nature of conditional utterances.
The passage ends with a fragment mentioning Imam al-Shafi'i.

It is permissible to expedite the fulfillment of a suspended vow. For example, a person says: 'If Allah cures the sick person, then I owe fasting three days.' So before the healing of the sick person, if he fasts three days, then the vow will be fulfilled, because the suspended has become a cause for obligation, although the obligation is delayed to the time of healing. For expediting (i.e., performing the act) after the existence of the cause before the obligation of performance is valid by consensus. Here, the performance of the vow is after the cause of obligation, so it is valid. Just as a person who has become owner of a nisab, if he pays zakat before the passing of a year, it is valid, because it is after the cause of obligation; the cause of obligation is ownership of nisab, which has occurred. Similarly, a vow is the cause of obligation, so fulfilling the suspended vow afterward is valid, because it is also after the cause. According to al-Shafi'i, the atonement for an oath?...

Commentary
This passage discusses the permissibility of fulfilling a suspended vow (nadhr mu'allaq) before the condition occurs, according to the principle established earlier. The example: a person says, 'If Allah cures the sick, then I owe fasting three days.' He may fast before the cure. The reasoning: the suspended utterance creates a cause (sabab) for the obligation of the vow, even though the actual obligation (wujub) is delayed until the condition (healing). Performing the act after the cause but before the obligation's completion is valid by consensus, similar to paying zakat after owning nisab but before the year passes. The passage ends with a reference to Imam al-Shafi'i on 'kaffarat al-yamin' (expiation for an oath), though the text is cut off. This 'tafri'' (branch) shows how the usul principle that the conditioned is a complete speech affects vows: the cause is established, so pre-fulfillment is valid. The Hanafi view would likely differ: since no complete speech exists until the condition, there is no cause, and pre-fulfillment would not be valid. The passage, however, presents the Shafi'i position as the basis for this ruling.

Key Terms
نذر معلق (nadhr mu'allaq): A vow suspended upon a condition, e.g., 'If Allah cures, I owe fasting'.
سبب الوجوب (sabab al-wujub): The cause that generates an obligation; here, the mere utterance of the vow creates a cause.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Metaphorical Use of the Command for Permission and Recommendation
Linguistic analysis of amr for ibaha/nadb

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the common factor (jami') that allows the command form to be used metaphorically for ibaha and nadb, and state why it is considered isti'ara rather than a deficient reality.

Core Idea
The command form used for permission or recommendation is metaphorical because permissibility of action links it to obligation.

In usul al-fiqh, we often encounter the command form (amr) used to convey not only obligation but also permission (ibaha) or recommendation (nadb). The question arises: is this a literal usage or a figurative one? This passage explores that question, focusing on the reasoning behind considering it isti'ara (metaphor) due to a shared element.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal-linguistic issue
The central issue: when the command form (amr) is used to mean ibaha or nadb, is it haqiqah (literal) or majaz (figurative)? The text notes a difference of opinion.
This is a key debate in usul al-fiqh about the scope of the command.
2
Extract the two positions
Some scholars (al-ba'd) say it is isti'ara (metaphor/majaz). Others imply it could be haqiqah qasirah (deficient reality). The text adopts the view of isti'ara.
Look for the phrase 'fist'ara at al-ba'd' in the Arabic.
3
Analyze the common factor (jami')
The common factor between the literal meaning (wujub, which includes permissibility of action and prohibition of omission) and the figurative meanings (ibaha/nadb, which have permissibility but no prohibition of omission) is the permissibility of action (jawaz al-fi'l).
This shared element establishes a relationship (alaqah) between the literal and figurative uses.
4
Apply the condition of isti'ara
For metaphorical usage, there must be a relationship (alaqah) linking the original meaning to the new context. Here, the shared permissibility of action serves as that relationship, making the usage metaphor.
The text explicitly says 'al-jami' jawaz al-fi'l wa 'alaqah jami'ah'.
5
Differentiate from 'ism al-kull 'ala al-ba'd'
The passage clarifies that this is not a case of applying the name of the whole to a part (ism al-kull). Instead, it is a true metaphor because the original meaning of amr includes both permissibility and prohibition of omission, whereas ibaha/nadb only have permissibility.
The phrase 'la itlaq ism al-kull 'ala al-ba'd' appears in the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسئله واذا اريد به الاباحة والندب الخ اس میں اختلاف ہے کہ جب امر سے مراد اباحہ یا ندب ہو تو یہ استعارہ ہو گا یعنی مجاز یا کہ یہ اطلاق علی طریق الحقیقۃ القاصرہ ہے تو مصنف نے اسے ذکر کرتے ہوئے کہا فاستعارة عند البعض والجامع جواز الفعل علاقہ جامعہ بین الحقيقة والمجاز جواز الفعل ہے یعنی امر کا حقیقی موضوع لہ وجوب تھا جس کا معنی ہے جواز الفعل والمنع عن الترک ندب وا باحتہ میں چونکہ جواز الفعل موجود ہے تو اسے معنی حقیقی کے ساتھ علاقہ قائم ہے اس لئے امر کا اطلاق ندب واباحتہ پر مجاز ہوگا ۔ لا اطلاق اسم الکل علی البعض یعنی یہاں اطلاق کل علی الجود نہیں
Issue: And when ibaha or nadb is intended by the command. There is a difference of opinion: when the command is used to mean ibaha or nadb, is it isti'ara (metaphor), i.e., majaz, or is it an application in the way of deficient reality (haqiqah qasirah)? The author, while mentioning it, said: 'So it is isti'ara according to some, and the common factor is the permissibility of the action. The relationship that is common between the haqiqah and the majaz is the permissibility of the action.' That is, the true subject of the command (haqiqi mawdu' lahu) was obligation, whose meaning is permissibility of the action and prohibition of omission. In nadb and ibaha, since permissibility of action is present, a relationship is established with the original meaning, so applying the command to nadb and ibaha is majaz. It is not the application of the name of the whole to the part (i.e., it is not a case of 'ism al-kull 'ala al-ba'd).
Source pages p. 437

Commentary
This passage discusses a linguistic-legal issue: When the command form (amr) is used to convey permission (ibaha) or recommendation (nadb), is that usage metaphorical (majaz/isti'ara) or does it represent a kind of deficient reality (haqiqah qasirah)? The author, following some scholars, considers it isti'ara. The common factor (jami') between the literal meaning (obligation, which includes the permissibility of action with prohibition of omission) and the figurative meaning (permission or recommendation, which includes permissibility without prohibition of omission) is the permissibility of the action (jawaz al-fi'l). Since this is shared, the command form is used metaphorically when intended for ibaha or nadb. The passage also clarifies that this is not a case of 'ism al-kull 'ala al-ba'd (using the term for the whole to refer to a part).

Key Terms
استعارہ (isti'ara): Metaphorical usage where a word is used for something other than its original meaning due to a relationship.
مجاز (majaz): Figurative language as opposed to haqiqah (literal).
حقیقت (haqiqah): Literal meaning of a word according to its original coinage.
جامع (jami'): The common factor that links the literal and figurative meanings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The prohibition of omission.
B) The permissibility of action (jawaz al-fi'l).
C) The demand for immediate action.
D) The praise for performing the action.

Key Takeaway
The command form used for permission or recommendation is metaphorical (isti'ara) due to the shared common factor of permissibility of action, distinguishing it from other figurative uses.

Linguistic Status of Amr Used for Nadb or Ibaha: Majaz or Haqiqah Qasirah?
Discusses whether using 'amr' for recommendation or permissibility is metaphorical or a limited literal usage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the positions of al-Karkhi/al-Jassas and Fakhr al-Islam on the classification of 'amr' when used for recommendation or permissibility, and apply this distinction in usul analysis.

Core Idea
Using 'amr' for recommendation or permission is not necessarily majaz—Fakhr al-Islam considers it a limited literal use (haqiqah qasirah).

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, the command form (amr) is primarily established for obligation. However, the same form is often used in the Quran and Sunnah to indicate recommendation (nadb) or permissibility (ibaha). This raises a linguistic question: is such usage metaphorical (majaz) or does it retain some literal quality? This passage delves into an intra-Hanafi debate on this issue, focusing on the concept of haqiqah qasirah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key terms
Define 'amr' (command), 'wujub' (obligation), 'nadb' (recommendation), and 'ibaha' (permissibility). Recognize that 'amr' has an established literal meaning (wujub).
2
State the dispute
Al-Karkhi and al-Jassas consider using 'amr' for nadb/ibaha as majaz. Others (including Fakhr al-Islam) consider it haqiqah qasirah.
3
Analyze the reasoning for majaz
The majaz view argues that nadb/ibaha are distinct (mubayin) from wujub, so applying the word 'amr' to them is using it for other than its original meaning.
4
Analyze the reasoning for haqiqah qasirah
The haqiqah qasirah view argues that nadb/ibaha include a part of the meaning of wujub (permissibility of the act), so the usage remains literal in a restricted sense.
5
Understand the tarjih
The author and Fakhr al-Islam favor the haqiqah qasirah view, as indicated by the phrase 'the author later confirmed the second as correct'.

Source Text

مجاز کی تعریف میں جو کہا گیا ہے کہ غیر ما وضع لہ کا ارادہ ہو اس غیر ما وضع لہ سے وہ معنی مراد ہے جو موضوع لہ سے خارج ہو اور جز پر لفظ غیر اطلاق نہیں ہوسکتا اور دلالتہ علی الجزیہ دلالتہ علی غیر ما وضع لہ نہیں ہے۔ اور غیر کی تعریف وہی جو علم الکلام میں مذکو یہ
Issue: And if permissibility or recommendation is intended by it, etc. There is disagreement regarding this: when by 'amr' permissibility or recommendation is meant, is it a metaphor (isti‘ara) i.e. figurative (majaz), or is it usage in the manner of a limited literal (haqiqah qasirah)? The author, while mentioning it, said: 'It is a metaphor according to some, and the common factor is the permissibility of the act. The connecting link between the literal and figurative is the permissibility of the act.' That is, the primary meaning of 'amr' was obligation, whose definition is 'permissibility of the act and prohibition of leaving it'. In recommendation and permissibility, since the permissibility of the act is present, there is a connection with the literal meaning. Therefore, applying 'amr' to recommendation and permissibility is figurative. 'Not the application of the name of the whole to the part.' That is, here it is not the application of the whole to the universal, as those who advocate limited literal say, because permissibility and recommendation are distinct from obligation. Indeed, the rulings such as obligation, recommendation, permissibility, etc., are mutually distinct rulings from each other. Therefore, a whole-part relationship cannot be established between them. Hence, permissibility is distinct from (mubayin) obligation; it is not a part of it. Know that there is a detailed disagreement: 'amr' is literal in obligation, i.e., its real intended meaning is obligation. So when, along with it, permissibility or recommendation is intended, that will be by way of metaphor, not literal, because it is using the word in other than what it was set for. That is, when by 'amr' permissibility or recommendation is meant, it is an intention other than what it was set for, so it will be figurative. Fakhr al-Islam has mentioned the disagreement in this issue. That is, that intending recommendation and permissibility being figurative is not agreed upon; rather there is disagreement in it. According to al-Karkhi and al-Jassas, it is figurative in them, i.e., using 'amr' for recommendation and permissibility is figurative. And according to some, it is literal in both, i.e., 'amr' is literal in recommendation and permissibility, not figurative. Note that according to them, it is not complete literal, but limited literal (haqiqah qasirah), i.e., using the word for some of what it was set for. And Fakhr al-Islam chose this, and the author later on confirmed the second as correct. Now what is the interpretation of this view that calls it literal, even though it is using in other than what it was set for? He said: that which is called 'majaz' in their terminology is a word by which a meaning is intended that is outside the set meaning. But when a part of the set meaning is intended by the word, then Fakhr al-Islam does not call it majaz; rather he names it 'haqiqah qasirah'. And that which indicates this terminology of Fakhr al-Islam is his statement: 'The meaning of permissibility and recommendation is a part of the meaning of obligation in estimation, i.e., originally.' Summarily: the meaning of recommendation and permissibility is a part of the meaning of obligation. Therefore, it is limited (qasir), not contradictory, so its indication to that meaning is a limited literal, not figurative. Figurative meaning is that which is contradictory to the set meaning. In the terminology of other scholars, majaz is a word by which other than what it is set for is intended, and 'other than what it is set for' is general, whether it is a part of the set meaning or a meaning outside the set meaning. And this definition is correct according to the commentator (Muttaqi?). But he (Fakhr al-Islam) restricts 'other than what it is set for' to the external meaning. That is, in the definition of majaz when it is said 'intention of other than what it is set for', by 'other than what it is set for' he means a meaning that is outside the set meaning, and he does not apply the word 'other' to a part, and indication to a part is not indication to other than what it is set for. And the definition of 'other' is the one mentioned in 'ilm al-kalam.
Source pages p. 437

Commentary
This passage is a usul definition and dispute: whether using the command form (amr) for meanings other than obligation—such as recommendation (nadb) or permissibility (ibaha)—is a metaphorical usage (majaz) or a limited literal usage (haqiqah qasirah). The author explains that the matn states it is a metaphor according to some, because the common factor (al-jami‘) is 'permissibility of the act' which is present in both obligation and these other meanings. However, it is not the application of the whole to a part because these meanings are distinct from obligation. The disagreement among Hanafi usulis is detailed: al-Karkhi and al-Jassas consider it majaz, while others (including Fakhr al-Islam) consider it haqiqah qasirah—a literal usage where the word is used for only a part of its original meaning. Fakhr al-Islam argues that the meaning of nadb and ibaha is a part of the meaning of obligation ('permissibility of the act' without 'prohibition of leaving'), so it is a limited literal usage. Other scholars define majaz as any use for other than the original meaning, including partial meanings. The passage clarifies this nuanced difference in terminologies within Hanafi usul al-fiqh.

Key Terms
حقيقة قاصرة (haqīqa qāṣirah): A literal usage where the word is applied to only a part of its original meaning, considered literal in a restricted sense and not metaphorical.
مجاز (majāz): A word used for a meaning other than its original, either completely external or (in broader definition) including partial meanings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Fakhr al-Islam's position explained in this passage, when the word 'amr' is used to indicate recommendation (nadb), it is classified as:
A) Majaz (metaphor).
B) Haqiqah qasirah (limited literal).
C) Haqiqah tammah (complete literal).
D) Ishtirak (homonym).

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the use of 'amr' for nadb or ibaha is not universally considered majaz; some scholars, including Fakhr al-Islam, classify it as haqiqah qasirah, a limited literal usage.

The Principle of Ahwat: Prioritizing Obligation Over Virtue
Applying the principle of ahwat to a missed vowed i'tikaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the principle of ahwat in Hanafi usul and apply it to determine the more precautionary ruling when an obligatory act conflicts with a recommended virtue.

Core Idea
When virtue risks losing obligation, ahwat commands securing obligation first.

In Hanafi legal theory, the principle of ahwat (precaution) guides the mufti when two rulings compete—one preserving a recommended virtue, the other fulfilling an obligation. This passage from Imam Fakhr al-Islam applies ahwat to a missed vowed i'tikaf. It demonstrates that when the recommended virtue (performing i'tikaf in Ramadan) may be lost due to death, fulfilling the obligation (the intended fasting) immediately is the more precautionary path. Understanding this principle sharpens your ability to derive rulings in complex scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conflict
Recognize the two competing considerations: the increase (ziyadah: virtue of performing i'tikaf in Ramadan) and the deficiency (nuqsan: lack of fasting in i'tikaf when done without fasting). The vowed i'tikaf was missed in Ramadan.
The increase is a recommended virtue; the deficiency is a lack of an obligatory component (fasting).
2
State the Hanafi principle of ahwat
Ahwat means the more precautionary ruling. In Hanafi usul, when there is a conflict between preserving a recommended virtue and fulfilling an obligation, the obligation takes precedence because it is primary (asl) and the virtue is secondary (tabi).
The principle is based on the idea that worship (ibadah) is the goal, and its completion is prioritized over extra merit.
3
Apply the principle to the case
The author argues that waiting for next Ramadan to gain the virtue of time is not obligatory due to the possibility of death. Since the increase (virtue of time) may fall away, the deficiency (lack of fasting) must be compensated immediately. Thus, qada with fasting becomes obligatory, and this is the ahwat ruling.
The reasoning uses the concept of 'bī tariq al-awla' (by priority): if the increase falls, the deficiency falls even more so because it is more important.
4
Understand the talil (rational proof)
The proof is that the falling away of deficiency (i.e., establishing the obligation of intended fasting) is worship, while the falling away of increase (virtue of time) is only a loss of a secondary merit. Therefore, ahwat requires that we ensure the obligation is fulfilled even if it means losing the virtue.
The author also notes that the virtue of time (Ramadan) is frequently lost because i'tikaf is valid in any month, but the virtue of intended fasting is rarely lost—only when i'tikaf is in Ramadan itself.
5
Note the ikhtilaf and conclusion
Some later commentators interpreted the two matters (one ahwat, one not) differently, but the author defends Fakhr al-Islam's original proof. The correct conclusion is that immediate qada with fasting is the mufta bihi (relied-upon) ruling.
This demonstrates the importance of returning to original usul proofs when faced with conflicting interpretations.

Source Text

In a case where a vowed i'tikaf was present but passed without i'tikaf, the obligation of observing that increase (the additional virtue of time) falls away. So the obligation of observing the increase in time and virtue drops, which was possible in one form: to wait for the next Ramadan. Because we mentioned the possibility of death — before the arrival of the next Ramadan, there is possibility of death, and the next Ramadan may not be attained. So it is befitting that that deficiency, which was compensated by that increase, should fall away. When this increase fell away, then along with it that deficiency also falls away by priority (bī tariq al-awla), which was being compensated by this increase. And that deficiency was the non-existence of the intended fasting. When this deficiency falls away, then the intended fasting specific to i'tikaf becomes obligatory. The aspect of priority is that in proving worship, caution (ihtiyat) is observed. So the falling away of deficiency is more important than the falling away of increase. Because with the falling away of deficiency, the intended fasting is established, which is worship. This is more important relative to the falling away of increase, i.e., the virtue of time. When it fell away, then the falling away of deficiency by the primary way is established, because with its falling away, the intended fasting, which is a primary worship, is obtained. Also, the second reason for this priority: the falling away of the increase of the virtue of time occurred because of the fear of death until the next Ramadan. The falling away of deficiency, i.e., the falling away of deficiency by which is meant that the intended fasting becomes obligatory, its proof is also due to the fear of death and also due to the vow of i'tikaf. When the mentioned increase has fallen away, which is due to one reason of falling away, then the mentioned deficiency falls away by priority, because its reasons for falling away are two. The falling away of deficiency is worship, and the falling away of deficiency is an expression, and its meaning is the obligation of intended fasting, i.e., the obligation of intended fasting in i'tikaf. So it is known that the falling away of the virtue of time (i.e., not waiting for the next Ramadan) establishes the obligation of intended fasting. And there is no doubt that the obligation of qada with the virtue of the intended fasting (i.e., the obligation of qada in such a way that with it the virtue of intended fasting is also obtained) is more precautionary (ahwat) than the obligation of qada with which only the virtue of the virtue of time is obtained. Because the virtue of the virtue of time is a virtue whose loss is predominant. That is, the virtue of time is a virtue whose loss is frequent, because i'tikaf is prescribed in all months of the year. Among the twelve months of the year, the virtue of time is only for the month of Ramadan. So most of the time of i'tikaf is empty from the virtue of the virtue of time, unlike the virtue of the intended fasting. And the loss of the virtue of the intended fasting is rare; that is only in the case when the vowed i'tikaf is in Ramadan, because then the fasting of Ramadan has virtue over the intended fasting. Apart from that, in all other months, the intended fasting in i'tikaf has virtue. This discussion is a form of a discussion from the principles of Imam Fakhr al-Islam. Some commentators have interpreted the two matters — one is more precautionary and the other is less precautionary — in a way different from the author's interpretation, which is mentioned in al-Talwih. So it should be looked at, but it is not hidden from those who are not deficient in intellect and have practice in the sciences that the proof that Fakhr al-Islam presented for the precautionary indicates that the meaning is what I mentioned, not what some commentators mistakenly thought. And praise be to Allah, the Inspirer.
Source pages p. 447

Commentary
This passage is a ruling and rational proof (talil) regarding the performance of a vowed i'tikaf that was missed in its specific time (Ramadan). The issue: A person vowed to observe i'tikaf in Ramadan but failed to do so. Should he wait for the next Ramadan to make it up (to gain the virtue of that time) or perform it earlier with fasting (but without the virtue of Ramadan)? The author argues that waiting for the next Ramadan is not obligatory because of the risk of death before that time. The principle used is that when an obligatory act cannot be performed with all its recommended virtues, the deficiency (lack of fasting in i'tikaf) is compensated by the increase (virtue of time). However, since the increase (the virtue of Ramadan) may be lost due to death, the deficiency (the intended fasting) must now be performed as obligatory, making qada with fasting more precautionary (ahwat). The author defends Fakhr al-Islam's view against other commentators, emphasizing that the proof for ahwat is clear.

Key Terms
احوط (ahwat): More precautionary; the ruling that is safer in terms of fulfilling the obligation, even if it means losing a recommended virtue.
زيادة (ziyadah): Increase; here refers to the additional virtue of performing i'tikaf in Ramadan as opposed to other months.
نقصان (nuqsan): Deficiency; here refers to the lack of fasting in i'tikaf when performed without fasting.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is performing qada of vowed i'tikaf with fasting (without waiting for next Ramadan) considered more precautionary (ahwat)?
A) Because the virtue of time (Ramadan) is easily lost and the intended fasting is a primary worship.
B) Because waiting for next Ramadan is itself a recommended act.
C) Because one should always delay qada to gain more reward.
D) Because the vow is only valid if performed in Ramadan.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi principle of ahwat requires that when an obligation may be lost due to delay, it must be fulfilled immediately, even at the cost of losing a recommended virtue.

Classification of Ada: Complete, Deficient, and Quasi-Qada
Defining the three types of performance (ada) and distinguishing them from qada

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any performance (ada) into ada mahd (kamil or qasir) or ada shabih bi al-qada, and justify the classification based on time and fulfillment of prescribed conditions.

Core Idea
Performance (ada) is either complete, deficient, or quasi-qada — the key is whether the act meets its prescribed conditions within the time.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, every commanded act (ma'mur bihi) is performed either as ada (on time) or qada (make-up). This lesson breaks down ada into three subcategories that depend on whether the act fulfills the conditions originally prescribed for it. Understanding this classification helps distinguish between a valid but imperfect performance and a proper qada.

Guided Path
1
Recall the ada-qada binary
Start by remembering that performance is either within the time (ada) or after it (qada). This lesson focuses on the subtypes within ada.
2
Identify the major division of ada
Ada is divided into 'pure performance' (ada mahd) and 'quasi-qada' (ada shabih bi al-qada). The latter looks like qada but is still within the time.
3
Subdivide pure ada into complete and deficient
Pure ada splits into 'complete' (kamil) when the act is performed exactly as prescribed (e.g., with congregation), and 'deficient' (qasir) when a prescribed condition is missing but the time is still valid (e.g., praying alone).
A masbuq (latecomer) who completes alone is also deficient because part of his prayer lacked congregation.
4
Understand quasi-qada through the lahiq example
A lahiq catches the beginning of congregational prayer but misses the rest due to sleep or hadath, then completes alone. His prayer is ada (within time) but resembles qada because he lost the commitment of following the imam.
This classification shows that not every act missing a condition is qada; it can still be a valid ada if done within time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و الاداء اما کامل پہلے گزر چکا ہے کہ مامور یہ کہ لانا یا تو بصورت ادا ہوگا یا بصورت قضا تو یہ اس کی تفصیل ہے پھر اداء دو قسم ہے ادار محض ۔ ادا شبیہ بالقضا۔ ادار محض دو قسم ہے ام کامل دهوان یودی کامل وہ ہے کہ ماموریہ کو اسی وصف و طریقہ پر ادا کیا جائے جس طرح وہ مشروع ہوا ہے کالجماعة یعنی صلوۃ مکتوبہ کی مشروعیتہ بوصف الجماعت ہے تو صلوۃ مکتوبہ کو بالحجامة ادا کرنا اداء کامل ہے او قاصر یہ ادا محض کی دوسری قسم ہے اداء قاصر ان لم یکن به اگر ادار وصف مشروعہ کے طور پر نہ ہو تو یہ ادائے قاصر ہے کصلوة المنفرد يعنى صلوة مكتوبه منفرداً ادا کرنا ادام قاصر ہے۔ اور اسی طرح مسبوقی جو بعد الامام منفردا ادا کرتا ہے یہ بھی ادائے قاصر ہے کیونکہ جميع صلوۃ کی ادائیگی با لوصف المشروع نہیں ہوتی ۔ او شبيه بالقضا یعنی اداء شبيه بالقضایه ارام محض کا قسیم ہے۔ کفعل اللاحق لاحق اس کو کہتے ہیں جو اول صلوۃ بالجماعۃ پائے اور باقی با لجماعت نہ ہو جیسے امام کے پیچھے نماز کے اندر مقتدی کو کھڑے ہوئے یا سحالت سجدہ وغیرہ نوم لاحق ہو جائے اور بعد از فراغت امام اسے انتباہ ہوا ہے یا امام کے پیچھے بعد الاقتد ا نماز کے دوران اسے حدث لاحق ہو گئی ہے جا کر اس نے وضو کیا جب واپس آیا تو امام فارغ ہو چکا ہے اب یہ لاحق ما بقی کو بعد فراغ الامام منفردا ادا کر رہا ہے یہ ادامہ شبیر بالقضا ہے فانه اداء باعتبار الوقت باعتبار اس کے کہ یہ فعل لاحق وقت کے اندر ہے تو ادا ہے اور باعتبار اس کے کہ جو اس نے اقتدا کے ساتھ التزام اداء مع الامام کیا تھا وہ فوت ہو گیا ہے تو شبیہ بالقضاء ہے خلاصہ
Performance (ada) is either complete. It has already passed that the commanded act (mu'ammar) is brought either in the form of ada or in the form of qada. So this is its detail. Then ada is of two types: pure ada (ada mahd) and quasi-qada (ada shabih bi al-qada). Pure ada is of two types: complete (kamil) — and they say 'yudi kamil' — it is that the commanded thing is performed in the same description and manner as it was prescribed, like congregation (jama'ah). For example, the prescribed prayer was prescribed with the description of congregation, so performing the prescribed prayer with congregation is complete ada. Or deficient (qasir) — this is the second type of pure ada: deficient ada is when it is not performed in the prescribed manner. For example, performing the prescribed prayer alone is deficient ada. Similarly, a latecomer (masbuq) who prays after the imam alone is also deficient ada, because not all of his prayer is performed with the prescribed description. Or quasi-qada (shabih bi al-qada) — it is the counterpart of pure ada. Like the action of the one who catches up (lahiq). Lahiq is the one who catches the first part of the prayer in congregation but the rest not in congregation, e.g., the follower behind the imam in prayer when he falls asleep while standing or during prostration etc., and after the imam finishes, he wakes up. Or behind the imam, after following, during the prayer he is overtaken by an event (hadath), goes and makes wudu, and when he returns the imam has finished. Now this lahiq performs the remainder after the imam's completion alone. This is quasi-qada: because it is ada in consideration of time (since this act is within the time), so it is ada, but in consideration that the commitment to perform with the imam (which he had by following) is lost, so it is similar to qada. End.
Source pages p. 448

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of the term 'ada' (performance) in usul al-fiqh. It begins by recalling that the commanded act (ma'mur bihi) is either performed as ada (on time) or qada (make-up). The author then divides ada into two major categories: Pure Ada (ada mahd) and Quasi-Qada (ada shabih bi al-qada). Pure Ada is further split into two: Complete (kamil) and Deficient (qasir). Complete ada is when the act is performed exactly as prescribed, fulfilling all recommended conditions. Example: performing the obligatory prayer (salat al-maktuba) in congregation (jama'ah), because the original prescription included congregation as a desirable condition. Deficient ada is when the act is performed within the time but without that prescribed condition, e.g., praying alone. Also, a masbuq (one who joins late) who completes his prayer after the imam is also deficient ada because part of his prayer lacked the congregation. Quasi-qada applies to a lahiq (one who catches the beginning but misses the rest due to sleep or hadath). He performs the remainder after the imam, within the time. This is technically ada (on time) but resembles qada because he lost the commitment of performing with the imam. This classification illustrates the Hanafi concepts of performance and the importance of prescribed conditions.

Key Terms
اداء محض (ada mahd): Pure performance; an act performed within its prescribed time with or without the prescribed conditions.
اداء شبیہ بالقضا (ada shabih bi al-qada): Quasi-qada; an act performed within the time but losing the condition of collective performance, thus resembling qada.
لاحق (lahiq): One who catches the beginning of the congregational prayer but misses the rest due to an excuse (sleep, hadath) and completes alone.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi classification, which of the following is considered ada shabih bi al-qada?
A) A person who prays Fajr alone at home after the time has started
B) A latecomer (masbuq) who completes his prayer after the imam finishes
C) A person who catches the beginning of congregational prayer but then falls asleep and completes alone after waking up
D) A person who prays Isha in congregation

Key Takeaway
Performance (ada) is defined by time, not by fulfillment of all conditions; thus ada includes complete, deficient, and quasi-qada forms.

Types of Qada' and Ada' in Hanafi Usul
Classifies performance and make-up into three types with examples from ibadat and mu'amalat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the three types of qada' and ada' (complete, deficient, and resembling performance) and apply the concept of qada' yushbihu al-ada' to both ibadat and mu'amalat.

Core Idea
When a missed act resembles the original location, it counts as actual performance, not make-up.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, the concepts of ada' (performance) and qada' (make-up) are not binary. The school identifies three categories: complete, deficient, and a third type—qada' that resembles ada'. This lesson explores how this third category works through the example of Eid takbirs said in ruku', and then extends the classification to huquq al-ibad with examples from usurpation, sale, and sarf/salam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of qada'
The passage distinguishes qada' bil-mithl al-ma'qul, qada' bil-mithl ghayr al-ma'qul, and qada' yushbihu al-ada'. The third type is explained as qada' that is similar to ada' due to resemblance.
The first two types are mentioned but not elaborated here.
2
Analyze the Eid takbir example
Someone joins the imam in ruku' during Eid prayer and says the extra takbirs in ruku' instead of qiyam. Even though the proper place (qiyam) is missed, the takbirs are considered ada', not qada', because ruku' resembles qiyam in uprightness of the lower body.
This shows that resemblance (shabah) can change the legal categorization from qada' to ada'.
3
Apply the same reasoning to huquq al-ibad
The passage gives examples of complete performance: returning the very thing in usurpation (same condition), delivering the sold item as contracted, and in sarf/salam where the delivered item is treated as the very obligation to avoid substitution before receipt. Deficient performance occurs when the returned item has added defects, e.g., a usurped slave commits a crime while with the usurper.
The Hanafi school uses these categories to define when an obligation is fully discharged and when compensation is due.
4
Understand the legal reasoning behind complete performance in sarf/salam
In sarf and salam, the obligation is a debt in the dhimmah. Delivering a specific item would normally be qada' (since the item is not the same as the debt). However, the Law treats the delivered item as the very obligation (ayn wajib) to prevent substitution before receipt (which is prohibited). Thus, it becomes complete performance.
This demonstrates how a secondary ruling (prohibition of substitution) shapes the primary classification.

Source Text

بدل الصرف اور بیع سلم میں مسلم فیہ کی تسلیم عین حق کی تسلیم ہے۔ لما عقد الصرف او السلم جب ایک شخص عقد صرف کرتا ہے یا عقد سلم تو بدل الصرف بیع صرف میں اور مسلم فیہ سلم میں واجب فی الذمہ ہو جاتی ہے جیسے الزمہ کا لئلا یکون استبدالاتاكه استبدال بدل صرف میں اور مسلم فیہ میں قبل القبض لازم نہ آئے جو کہ شرعاً حرام ہے اگر اس مودی کو عین واجب قرار نہ دیا جائے بلکہ اصلی واجب صرف وصف وجوب فی الذمہ ہو اور یہ موڈی اس کا بدل قرار دیا جائے تو به استبدال قبل القبض لازم آئین گا جو کہ حرام ہے لہذا موری عین واجب ہے اس کی ادائیگی ادائے کامل ہو گی ۔ والقاصر كرد المغصوب والمبيع مشغولا الخير حقوق العباد میں ادام قاصر کی مثال ہے ایک غاصب نے ایک عبد غصب کیا جو فارغ از عیوب تھا لیکن جب واپس کیا تو یہ مشغولا بالجنایت ہے مثلا کسی کو اس غلام نے قتل کیا یا کسی کا ہاتھ کاٹا یا مشغول بالدین ہے مثلاً اس نے کسی کا مال تباہ کیا جس کی ضمان اسے لاحق ہے یا مریض ہوا اور یہ تمام اشیا بدست غاصب ہوئی ہیں اسی طرح جاریہ غصب کی جو غیر حاملہ تھی جب واپس کی تو وہ حاملہ ہے اور یہ حمل بدست غاصب ہوا تو یہ ادائے قاصر ہے اسی طرح کسی بائع نے عبد یا جاریہ کی بیع کی بوقت بیع کے مبیع سالم از عیوب تھی جب مشتری نے بعد میں قبض کیا تو مبیع ان عیوب مذکورہ میں سے کسی عیب کے ساتھ معیب
And as for qada' that resembles ada' (performance), this is the third type. The first two types are qada' by a rational equivalent and qada' by a non-rational equivalent, and the third is this qada' that resembles ada', i.e., qada' that is similar to performance. Like: when a person finds the imam in the bowing position in Eid prayer, and in that state he follows and says the extra takbirs in the state of bowing, even though the original place for these extra takbirs is missed—it was standing—and there is no qada' for Eid takbirs, because in terms of closeness and worship, there is no equivalent for them. But because bowing resembles standing due to the remaining uprightness and straightness in the lower half of the body, so saying the takbirs while bowing is as if they were said in standing, so this is similar to performance. And the rights of people (huquq al-ibad) are also divided into these categories; within them occur complete performance, deficient performance, and what resembles ada', and the three types of qada'. Complete performance in huquq al-ibad: some examples: returning the very thing in usurpation, i.e., the usurper returns the very thing he usurped, and it is in the same condition and description as at the time of usurpation. And in sale, i.e., delivering the very thing in sale, i.e., the seller hands over the sold item in the same condition as at the time of the sale contract. And in sarf and salam, i.e., delivering the very thing in the contract of sarf and salam: in sarf sale, the countervalue of sarf, and in salam sale, the commodity (muslam fihi) is delivery of the very right. Because when a person makes a sarf contract or a salam contract, the countervalue in sarf and the commodity in salam become an obligation in the dhimmah, like a debt, its equivalent becomes obligatory in the dhimmah. And this obligation in the dhimmah is a description that does not admit of delivery and handover. Therefore, giving the countervalue of sarf and the commodity in place of this description should be qada', because the very thing (countervalue/commodity) is other than the debt, i.e., what was obligatory in the dhimmah was a debt, and this very thing is other than it, so it should be qada'. But the Law (shar') made this delivered thing the very obligation (ayn wajib) in place of that obligation in the dhimmah, to avoid substitution before receipt, which is prohibited. If this delivered thing is not considered the very obligation, but the original obligation is only the description of being in the dhimmah, and this delivered thing is its substitute, then substitution before receipt would occur, which is prohibited. Therefore, the delivered thing is the very obligation, and its payment is complete performance (ada' kamil). And deficient performance: like returning the usurped or sold item while it is occupied with something. In huquq al-ibad, an example of deficient performance: a usurper usurped a slave that was free from defects, but when he returned it, it was occupied with a crime, e.g., the slave killed someone or cut off someone's hand, or occupied with a debt, e.g., he destroyed someone's property for which compensation is due, or became sick, and all these occurred at the hand of the usurper. Similarly, a slave girl who was non-pregnant when usurped, when returned she is pregnant, and this pregnancy occurred at the hand of the usurper. This is deficient performance. Similarly, a seller sold a slave or slave girl, at the time of sale the sold item was free from defects, when the buyer later took possession, the sold item was defective with one of the mentioned defects.
Source pages p. 453

Commentary
This passage from a Hanafi usul commentary explains the categories of ada' (performance) and qada' (make-up) in huquq al-Allah (rights of Allah) and huquq al-ibad (rights of people). The author first introduces a third type of qada': qada that resembles ada' (qada' yushbihu al-ada'). This is explained through the example of someone joining the Eid prayer in ruku' and saying the extra takbirs in ruku' instead of qiyam. Although the proper time for takbirs (standing) is missed, the ruku' is similar to standing in some aspects, so saying takbirs in ruku' is treated as if it were actual performance, not a make-up. This shows that similarity can affect the legal categorization. Then the author applies the same logic to huquq al-ibad, giving examples of complete performance (returning exactly what was taken, e.g., usurper returns the same item in same condition; seller delivers same item; in sarf/salam, delivering the very thing is considered complete performance to avoid riba-related prohibition of substitution before receipt). Deficient performance examples: returning a slave or slave girl with added defects occurring at the hand of the usurper, e.g., the slave commits a crime or becomes sick. These are considered deficient (qasir) because the obligation is not fully discharged. The passage demonstrates how the Hanafi school applies these conceptual categories derived from ibadat to mu'amalat.

Key Terms
قضاء يشبه الأداء (qada' yushbihu al-ada'): A type of make-up that is similar to actual performance due to resemblance in some attributes.
أداء قاصر (ada' qasir): Deficient performance where the delivered item has a defect or extra liability incurred during the hand of the liable person.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) To make the transaction easier for the parties.
B) To avoid the prohibition of substitution before receipt (استبدال قبل القبض).
C) Because the delivered item is identical to the debt.
D) Because the schools of law disagree on this point.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes three categories of performance and make-up, with the unique category of 'qada' resembling ada' applied based on legal resemblance (shabah).

The Usurper's Defective Return: Hanafi View on Discharge
Hanafi rebuttal against the view that a usurper who deceives is not discharged

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule of adā' to cases where a usurper returns property with deception, and distinguish between valid discharge and incomplete performance.

Core Idea
Returning the very property discharges liability even if defective; the owner's subsequent act is decisive.

This lesson examines a specific usul al-fiqh dispute: does a usurper who returns property through deception (e.g., mixing it with his own food) escape liability? The Hanafi school argues that the actual return of the 'ayn constitutes a valid adā', regardless of the deception, because the owner's voluntary consumption is an independent cause of loss. We will analyze the opposing argument and the Hanafi response based on the text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed ruling
The passage presents two positions: an unnamed opponent holds that the usurper is not discharged because he was ordered to return the property, not to deceive. The Hanafi position (with 'lana') asserts he is discharged because the property itself is returned.
2
Extract the opposing proof
The opponent argues: (a) The usurper was commanded to return the exact object, and deception violates that command. (b) The owner might eat more than his own property, so the deception causes loss beyond the usurped item.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafis respond: The return is truly an 'adā' because the object returned is the very property of the owner. The owner's act of eating is his own choice; he destroys his own property. Ignorance is not an excuse for the owner, so the usurper's liability ends.
4
Evaluate the dismissal of the counter-argument
The opponent's point about the owner eating more than his share is dismissed as 'futile' because it assumes dishonesty, which is contrary to the presumption of honesty. Thus, it has no effect on the discharge.
5
Apply the rule to similar cases
Consider: what if the usurper returns the property damaged? The Hanafi principle suggests that as long as the substance is returned, adā' is valid. The owner's acceptance (even if forced) and subsequent use may determine liability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نزدیک برخی از ضمان نہیں ہوگا ۔ لانه مامور بالاداء المبلغ کیونکہ غاصب شرعا مامور تھا کہ وہی شئی مغصوبہ واپس کرے فریب دینے کا مامور نہیں تھا ور بما یاکل یعنی کبھی انسان مقام اباحہ میں اپنے مال کی مقدار سے زیادہ کھا جاتا ہے اور کبھی کم اس لئے اس فریب دہی سے بری از ضمان نہیں ہوگا۔ ولنا انه اداء حقیقۃ ہماری دلیل یہ ہے کہ یہ حقیقتاً ادا ہے کیونکہ مالک کی شئی بعینہ اس نے واپس کر دی ہے اگر چہ اس ادا میں قصور تو ضرور ہے لیکن جب مالک نے کھا لیا اس نے اپنے اختیار کے ساتھ اتلاف کیا ہے لہذا ادا تام ہو جائیگی جہل اور ناعلمی کوئی عذر نہیں ہے ۔ باقی رہی یہ بات کہ انسان مقام اباحت میں اپنے مال سے زیادہ کھا جاتا اور یہ عادت مخالف دیانت ہے لہذا اس کے ساتھ غاصب بری از ضمان نہ ہو یہ ایک لغو بات ہے لہذا اس کا ادا پر کوئی اثر نہیں ہوگا۔
According to some, he will not be liable for guarantee. Because he was ordered to return the exact usurped item, he was not ordered to deceive. And because he eats according to his capacity, sometimes he eats more than his property and sometimes less, so due to this deception he will not be discharged from liability. And for us: it is truly a discharge. Our proof is that he actually returned the very property of the owner, although there was a fault in this return. But when the owner ate it, he destroyed it with his own choice, so the discharge becomes complete. Ignorance and unawareness are no excuse. As for the argument that a person in a permissible situation eats more than his property and this is contrary to honesty, so with this the usurper is not discharged from liability – this is a futile statement, so it has no effect on the discharge.
Source pages p. 457

Commentary
This passage presents a Hanafi legal argument regarding the discharge of a usurper (ghāṣib). The text first states an opposing view: a usurper who deceives the owner into eating the usurped property (e.g., by mixing it with his own food) is not discharged from liability because he was ordered only to return the property, not to deceive, and because the owner's consumption may exceed his own property. The Hanafi response (with 'lana') asserts that the return is truly an 'adā' (discharge) because the very property is returned. Although there is deception, the owner's voluntary act of consuming it is an independent destruction, so the usurper is freed. The counterargument that the owner might eat more than his own share is dismissed as contrary to honesty and irrelevant. The commentary emphasizes that the key is the actual return of the 'ayn (specific property), even if the means is defective. This ruling aligns with the Hanafi principle that 'adā' is valid when the object is delivered, regardless of the accessory fault, as the owner's subsequent action is his own responsibility.

Key Terms
اداء (adā'): The act of discharging a liability by delivering the very thing owed (the 'ayn).
ضمان (ḍamān): Liability for compensation (guarantee) for the loss of property.
غاصب (ghāṣib): A usurper who wrongfully takes and holds another's property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, why is the usurper (ghāṣib) discharged from liability when he returns the property even with deception?
A) Because the usurper is ordered to return the property, and deception is not prohibited.
B) Because the owner eats the property voluntarily, which is an independent act of destruction.
C) Because the owner's ignorance excuses the usurper.
D) Because the usurper's deception makes the property ownerless.

Key Takeaway
The usurper's liability is discharged upon returning the very property, regardless of deception; the owner's voluntary consumption severs liability.

Liability for Loss of Qisas: The Usul of Non-Monetary Rights
Expands a fiqh ruling to reveal its usul basis

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle that a right without a monetary equivalent (la mithl lahu) cannot be guaranteed by wealth, using the example of witnesses retracting in qisas.

Core Idea
Qisas is a pure meaning (ma'na) with no monetary equivalent; its loss cannot be made good by wealth.

This passage from al-Tawzih wa al-Talwih is not merely a fiqh ruling about witness liability; it is a window into the Hanafi usul principle that distinguishes between tangible property (a'yan) and abstract meanings (ma'ani). Understanding this distinction is key to Hanafi rulings on liability and compensation, particularly in cases where the right lost is not property but an abstract right like qisas.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling from the passage
State the two specific fiqh rulings given: witnesses who retract are not liable, and a non-relative who executes the qatil is not liable.
The ruling is negative: no financial guarantee.
2
Extract the reasoning (ta'lil)
Find the key phrase 'استیفاء قصاص ایک معنی ہے جس کی کوئی مثل معقول نہیں ہے' (qisas is a meaning that has no rational equivalent). This is the core ta'lil.
The reasoning is that qisas is a ma'na, not a 'ayn (physical object).
3
Identify the usul category
Classify the right of qisas under the Hanafi dichotomy of a'yan vs. ma'ani. Ma'ani are abstract rights that cannot be quantified or replaced by wealth.
Compare with property (mal) which has mithl and can be compensated.
4
Apply the principle to a hypothetical
Consider a Shafi'i view that might impose diyah. Use the Hanafi principle to explain why diyah is not an equivalent of qisas—diyah is a separate right, not a replacement.
This shows that the absence of mithl ma'qul blocks monetary guarantee.
